Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model

2 Abstract 
Atherosclerosis is a chronic inflammation linked to obesity and the third most fatal disease world-
wide. The growing obese population and the current lack of treatment specifically targeting athero-
sclerosis give rise to efforts in the research society focusing on understanding biological mecha-
nisms in developing atherosclerosis such as investigating the regulation of certain 𝛾-carboxyglu-
tamic acid (Gla) proteins related to atherosclerosis. All Gla proteins comprise certain glutamic acid 
(Glu ) residues that undergo vitamin K dependent (VKD) 𝛾-carboxylation. The degree of 𝛾-carbox-
ylation affects the activity and function of the Gla proteins being Matrix Gla Protein (MGP), Oste-
ocalcin (OC), and Unique Cartilage Matrix Associated Protein (UCMA). MGP is expressed by vas-
cular smooth muscle cells (VSMCs) in blood vessels and soft tissue, MGP is both 𝛾-carboxylated 
and serine-phosphorylated to become fully activated in terms of binding to Ca!"-ions and prevent 
calcification in atherosclerotic plaques. Atherosclerosis comprises calcified plaque lesions, and lo-
cal tissue at the atherosclerotic lesion might respond by regulating the expression of MGP, hence 
investigation of the regulation of MGP. Moreover, studies in literature have found that VSMCs 
might transdifferentiate into osteoblast/chondrocyte phenotype expressing OC and UCMA. OC is 
usually expressed in bone tissue by osteoblasts, and UCMA in cartilage by chondrocytes. The trans-
differentiation of VSMCs give rise to investigate the regulation of OC and UCMA in tissue which 
might be affected by obesity-induced atherosclerosis. The Ossabaw miniature pig is an ideal transla-
tional model to investigate the regulation of Gla-proteins in relation to obesity-induced atheroscle-
rosis. This is attributed to the high correlation of the pathogenesis between human and Ossabaw 
pigs when pigs are introduced to a metabolic syndrome (MetS) diet, which induces vascular calcifi-
cation and diffuse atherosclerosis lesions. 
 
This Master thesis aimed to examine the regulation of the Gla proteins; MGP, OC, and UCMA in 
response to obesity utilizing tissue samples from the Ossabaw miniature pigs comprising 3 obese 
and 4 lean pigs. The protein expression levels of OC were examined to investigate OC’s role as po-
tential biomarker to detect atherosclerosis. Primer pairs targeting MGP, OC and UCMA deoxyribo-
nucleic acid (DNA) were designed to be used in Reverse-transcriptase quantitative polymerase 
chain reaction (RT-qPCR) assays together with complementary DNA (cDNA) samples obtained 
from lean and obese subcutaneous adipose tissue (SAT), PAX stabilized blood samples (PAX), 
heart, liver and aorta tissue. The relative quantity (RQ) of target DNA across cDNA samples ob-
tained by RT-qPCR gave insight in the expression levels of the target DNA, hence the up- or down-
regulation in response to obesity. It was solely possible to obtain specific polymerase chain reaction 
(PCR)-products in the RT-qPCR assays targeting MGP, which indicated that tissue-specific up- or 
downregulation of MGP was affected by obesity. The RT-qPCR assays targeting OC and UCMA 
did not provide specific PCR products, hence the regulation could not be studied further in this the-
sis. An Enzyme-Linked Immunosorbent Assay (ELISA) targeting OC in plasma samples obtained 
from whole blood, and SAT, liver and heart tissue extracts did not suggest OC to be a strong bi-
omarker to detect atherosclerosis, since the protein levels did not differ between obese and lean 
samples. It should be mentioned that there was no guarantee that the obese pigs had developed ath-
erosclerosis, especially since the pigs were solely placed on a short term MetS diet.

3 Introduction  
3.1 Atherosclerosis  
Atherosclerosis is a chronic inflammatory disease where formation of plaque lesions occur within 
the inner side of the arteries, which are responsible of leading oxygenated blood from the heart to 
the rest of the body[1]. Atherosclerosis oversees the greatest proportion of fatal events caused by 
cardiovascular disease globally. Multiple parameters can initiate the development of atherosclerosis 
such as diet, lack of exercise, stress, obesity, genetic factors, amongst others. Atherosclerotic plaque 
formation can initiate at an early age and progress in silence for years before a potentially fatal 
event occurs, which means that several age groups are affected by the disease. The impact of ather-
osclerosis extends beyond the patients since the disease compiles both a social and economic bur-
den of the health care systems on a global level[2]. Currently, the treatment consists of lifestyle 
changes, medication in form of blood pressure drugs and anti-blood clotting medication or stents 
inserted by surgery. However, there is no medication targeting the specific atherosclerotic develop-
ment or existing plaques[3]. Consequently, scientific efforts are continuously investigated with the 
purpose of addressing risk factors and examining the mechanisms in development of atherosclerotic 
plaque formation and thereby identify potential targets as biomarkers or as treatment. Last men-
tioned has led to an increased interest in certain vitamin K dependent proteins (VKDP), which have 
shown to inhibit calcification in atherosclerotic plaque formation. This master thesis will further ex-
amine the potential regulation of certain VKDP in relation to the risk factor being obesity. How-
ever, firstly the current known details concerning the blood vessels anatomy and atherosclerotic 
plaque formation will be further described in the following subsections. 
 
3.1.1 The Composition of The Blood Vessel Wall  
The artery wall is composed of three layers, referred to as tunics, which are illustrated in Figure 3.1. 
The inner layer is named tunica intima which consists of endothelial cells (EC) and connective tis-
sue layers, which are held together by the basement membrane. The basement membrane is permea-
ble, which allows various molecules to differentiate between the layers of tunics. Moreover, the 
basement membrane supports the tunica intima with flexibility and strength. The second layer is 
named tunica media, which includes layers of VSMCs kept together by elastic fibers. VSMCs are 
responsible for providing structural integrity and flexibility in terms of regulating the diameter of 
the blood vessel in response to blood flow and vasoactive stimuli[4]. A layer of collagenous elastic 
fibers holds the tunica media together with the tunica intima, and the third layer named tunica ex-
terna. Tunica externa is made up of collagenous and elastic fibers, whilst the outer area of the tunica 
externa supports keeping the artery at its right position by incorporating connective tissue from the 
adjacent tissue. In addition, the tunica externa of larger vessels is equipped with further small ves-
sels to provide nutrients, oxygen amongst others to the cells in the tunic externa. The small vessels, 
named vasa vasorum, are essential, since diffusion of nutrients from the main vessel to the outer tu-
nics is challenged by the overall thicker walls of the large vessels[5]. Lastly, the tunica externa in-
cludes nerves, called nervi vasorum. The nervi vasorums function to control VSMCs in performing 
==PAGE:7==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
6 
vasoconstriction and vasodilation. The composition of the blood vessel wall in veins is made to 
manage a lower blood pressure than arteries, wherefore components as the elastic fibers between 
the tunics are absent, and some veins possess valves to support the uniform direction of the blood 
stream. Moreover, the intima is thinner due to reduced pressure, the media comprise vasa vasorum 
since the reduced pressure allows them to position closer to the lumen, and the externa is thicker in 
veins and comprises VSMCs[5]. An illustration of the composition of both arteries and veins is de-
picted in Figure 3.1. 
 
Figure 3.1. Composition of the artery and vein blood vessel wall [5]. 
3.1.2 Progression of Atherosclerotic Lesions 
Atherosclerotic plaque formation occurs when the ECs within the tunica intima are disrupted in 
their maintenance of vascular homeostasis due to alterations in the concentrations of metabolic fac-
tors in the blood stream or mechanical stress[1]. The dysfunction of ECs promotes monocyte re-
cruitment to the area and reduces the amount of nitric oxide (NO) inducing VSMC migration into 
the tunica intima.  
The progress of an atherosclerotic plaque formation is initiated by the endothelial dysfunction, 
which will lead to low-density-lipoprotein (LDL) infiltration in the tunica intima[1]. LDL is referred 
to as the ‘bad’ cholesterol, since it is recognized to aggregate in arteries leading to atherosclerotic 
plaque formation[6]. LDL infiltration is more prone to aggregate at sites with disrupted blood flow, 
which could be a result of blood vessels separating. Starting from a single vessel, the blood flow 
will exist in a streamlined flow with a moderate wall shear stress (WSS). The blood flow will then 
enter the splitting region before entering two individual vessels. The area of separation will affect 
the blood flow to flow in a turbulent mode with a low WSS before entering two separate blood ves-
sels, with streamlined blood flows[1].  
The turbulent blood flow and decreased WSS will support LDL infiltration, leading to endothelial 
dysfunction. However, the increased undisturbed blood flow and WSS in straight arteries enhances 
the upregulation of endothelial NO synthase (eNOS), which increases NO production. NO supports 
glucose clearance, reduces triglycerides, inhibits migration of VSMCs, and can diffuse into the tu-
nica media and affect the VSMCs to relax and thereby induce vasodilation, amongst others. How-
ever, inhibition of eNOS will result in reduced production of NO, which will increase the likeliness 
==PAGE:8==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
7 
of cardiovascular and metabolic effects including endothelial dysfunction leading to VSMCs mi-
grating into the tunica intima[1].  
The infiltrated LDLs at the tunica intima may become oxidized by free radicals or enzymes as e.g., 
phospholipases[7]. Oxidized LDLs act as stimulators activating the innate immune system to recruit 
monocytes to the area. The recruited monocytes move along the ECs by rolling and migrate into the 
tunica intima where they differentiate into macrophages. The macrophages consume the oxidized 
LDL in the intima, however a proportion of the migrated VSMCs in the tunica intima can take up 
LDL by certain receptors. Macrophages and certain VSMCs taking up LDL promote the creation of 
foam cells. The continuous recruitment of VSMCs and monocytes differentiating into macrophages 
migrating to the tunica intima will create a large proportion of foam cells. The foam cells will lead 
to the establishment of a fatty streak, which is known as the first sign of plaque formation[8]. The 
accumulated dead foam cells and enriched lipid area in the tunica intima is referred to as the ne-
crotic core. The necrotic core is enclosed by a fibrotic cap made from elastin, collagen and other ex-
tracellular matrix material created by another differentiated phenotype of VSMC, called synthetic 
VSMCs. The fibrotic cap encloses the plaque lesion to avoid thrombotic events, which could follow 
by exposing substances from the necrotic core to the circulation[1].  
Arterial plaque calcification is the next phase, which covers the formation of calcium phosphate 
crystals. The formation and accumulation of calcium phosphate crystals occur in correlation with 
released matrix vesicles (MV) because of macrophages and VSMCs which have undergone apopto-
sis. The MVs can differ in their internal composition, however they are found to act as a nucleating 
foci of calcification [9]. In response to calcification, macrophages may be triggered to release pro-
inflammatory cytokines as TNF-alpha amongst others, which initiates osteo/chondrogenic transdif-
ferentiating of VSMCs[10], [11]. Osteoblasts are found in bone tissue and referred to as bone-form-
ing cells; chondrocytes maintain the cartilage tissue. The osteo/chondrogenic VSMCs produce min-
eralized matrix in the plaque lesions, thus driving the growth of the calcified area[1]. The final 
phase is an eventual bursting of the plaque lesion which could lead to severe consequences or po-
tentially fatal events. Figure 3.2 illustrates the phases in atherosclerosis progression.  
 
Figure 3.2. Atherosclerosis progression (made in BioRender).

3.2 Gla Proteins 
Gla proteins are a group of VKDP containing different numbers of Glu residues, which are capable 
of being 𝛾-carboxylated into Gla domains during the post-translational modification. VKDPs can be 
divided in classes with different functions: Coagulation factors e.g., prothrombin, factor VII, IX & 
X; Anti-coagulation factors e.g., protein C, S and Z; Extrahepatic Gla proteins, which can prevent 
calcification in both the vascular system and cartilage[12]. A brief overview of the Gla proteins in-
vestigated in this master thesis is found below in Table 3.1, in addition a comprehensive description 
will be presented in the following subsections. It is essential to note that the majority of protein en-
tries presented in the following subsection are labeled as “UniProtKB reviewed (Swiss-Prot)”, how-
ever solely when it was possible. The entries within the Swiss-Prot section of UniProtKB have all 
been examined manually by UniProt curators. UniProtKB/Swiss-Prot is a high-quality database, 
which have collected and combined experimental data, scientific literature, sequence analysis, evi-
dence attribution, etc., into every entry. The unreviewed entries of UniProtKB are located in the 
TrEMBL section, which comprises entries that are automatically processed and reviewed by com-
putational analysis, due to an increased flow of data from genomic projects [13], [14].

3.2.1 Matrix Gla Protein (MGP) 
MGP is a secretory protein produced by VSMCs in the tunica media of blood vessels, endothelial 
cells in the heart, kidney & lungs and chondrocytes residing in cartilage[15]. It serves as an inhibi-
tor of calcification in the circulation and extracellular matrix (ECM), due to its activity in regard to 
binding Ca2+ ions [20]. However, the activity of MGP relies on post-translational modifications 
comprising phosphorylation of Serine (Ser)-residues and VKD 𝛾-carboxylation of Glu-residues at 
specific locations. The p-cMGP possess an increased negative charge, in comparison to the dp-
ucMGP. Resulting in that p-cMGP has increased affinity to binding Ca2+ ions, thereby inhibiting 
them to join into hydroxyapatite [20]. Hydroxyapatite is known to be present in bone tissue and to 
be a common component in vascular calcification. In contrast, secreted dp-ucMGP does not possess 
a known active role in either the ECM or the systemic circulation and is therefore referred to as ‘the 
inactive form of MGP’[20]. However, dp-ucMGP may be a relevant biomarker since an increased 
concentration of dp-ucMGP in plasma may reflect insufficient levels or lack of VK. Lack of VK 
could indicate decreased levels of active VKDP including p-cMGP which are then not able to pre-
vent calcification, leading to atherosclerosis, artery stiffness amongst other[21].  
Even though dp-ucMGP can be secreted, then there may be a possibility that there might be lack of 
checkpoints during the post-translational modification ensuring completion of 𝛾-carboxylation and 
phosphorylation. Potentially, dp-ucMGP may have an unidentified function. It is known that the 
VKD 𝛾-carboxylation of Glu-residues are performed in the endoplasmic reticulum (ER), and the 
phosphorylation of the Ser-residues in the Golgi apparatus, independently from one another [20]. 
Aforementioned, give rise for 2 other conformations of MGP to exist as dp-cMGP & p-ucMGP, and 
potentially secreted, since dp-ucMGP are capable of being secreted. However, the information con-
cerning these conformations of MGP remains largely unknown. Figure 3.3 illustrates MGPs path 
into the systemic circulation and/or ECM, which depends on the VKD modifications during the 
post-translational modification.  
==PAGE:11==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766)

Figure 3.3. An overview of MGPs VKD post-translational modification of Glu- and Ser-residues and further path into

3.2.1.1 The Structure of MGP 
The primary structure of MGP within Homo sapiens (H. sapiens) & Sus scrofa (S. scrofa), including 
the signal peptide and propeptide is 103 aa residues. The signal peptide sequence is found at the N-
terminal at position 1-19. The UniProt database states that the propeptide in MGP is found near the 
C-terminal, at position 97-103, in H. sapiens[22]. The database of UniProt does not have data con-
cerning the length or location of the propeptide in S. scrofa, however the similarities found in the 
primary sequence of MGP between H. sapiens & S. scrofa could lead to the assumption that the 
length and location of the propeptide would remain the same in the two species. The primary se-
quence with details concerning amino acid composition and alignment of the MGP in H. sapiens 
and S. scrofa is found in Figure 3.4. 
 
Figure 3.4. The primary structure of MGP in H. sapiens (P08493) & S. scrofa (Q8MJ39) [22], [23].  
The lengths of P08493 & Q8MJ39 and all positions of Cys-, and Glu-residues are conserved, while it is solely 4 out of 5 
Ser-residues which are conserved. However, all PTM sites are conserved. 
Phosphorylation 
𝛾-carboxylation 
==PAGE:12==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
11 
MGP is a 10.6 kDa VKDP consisting of 84 amino acid (aa) residues in H. sapiens (P08493). The 
84 aa residue length of the MGP protein is conserved between P08493 and S. scrofa (Q8MJ39), due 
to the assumption of the signal- and propeptide lengths and locations being identical, as abovemen-
tioned. However, the molecular weight (MW) of Q8MJ39 is not specified, however due to the high 
homology between the two species it is further assumed to be similar to 10.6 kDa. Figure 3.5 illus-
trates the alignment of P08493 & Q8MJ39 performed in EMBOSS Needle, which provides a de-
tailed insight in which alterations are found in MGP between the two species.  
 
 
 
Figure 3.5. EMBOSS Needle Alignment of P08493 and Q8MJ39, with BLOSUM62 Matrix (Appendix 1). 
The vertical bar represents identical aa residue matches. The colon represents conservative substitutions, meaning 
strong similarity. The dot represents semi-conservative substitutions, meaning weak similarity.  
 
The EMBOSS Needle alignment, in Figure 3.5, identified 17 aa residue alterations out of 103 aa 
residues, which means that there is a positive alignment of 83.5% identical aa residues between the 
2 species. The alterations consist of 10 conservative substitutions, and 7 of them being semi-con-
servative, which means that the similarity of MGP in the 2 species is 93.2% (Appendix 1). The high 
similarity validates that MGP is greatly conserved between the 2 species. Although, some of the 
semi-conservative substitutions may interfere with the structure of MGP, and thereby the structure 
of the protein in the 2 species might differ slightly. 4 out of 7 semi-conservative substitutions are 
found in the signal- and propeptide region, leaving 3 out of 7 semi-conservative substitutions in the 
coding region of the mature MGP.  
Focusing on the coding region for the mature protein the substitution of hydrophobic Valine (V) in 
P08493 to the hydrophobic Alanine (A) in Q8MJ39 at position 54, might not alter the structure 
much since both aas are found in the same subtype of aas with hydrophobic side chains. However, 
A is a smaller aa with a less branched side chain, which may lead to decreased hydrophobic packag-
ing, in Q8MJ39. The substitution of positive charged Arginine (R) in P08493 to the hydrophobic 
Leucine (L) in Q8MJ39 at position 61, changes the charge locally. The substitution of hydrophobic 
V in P08493 to the aromatic Proline (P) in Q8MJ39 at position 65, might disrupt H-bonds due to 
P’s lacking H-atom in its amino group due to its cyclic structure. This can result in destabilization 
of  α-helix structures, due to the loss of H-atom donation from P[24]. The semi-conservative aa sub-
stitutions found in the coding sequence of the mature MGP in both species are displayed in Figure 
3.6.

3.2.2 Osteocalcin (OC) 
OC is a non-collagenous secretory protein synthesized in bone by osteoblasts, which are also re-
ferred to as bone-forming cells. The synthesis of OC in the osteoblasts comprises VKD 𝛾-carboxy-
lation of specific Glu residues with cOC as outcome. After synthesis, cOC is secreted into the bone 
extracellular matrix, where it facilitates bone formation by binding to Ca2+ ions and supporting their 
incorporation into the bone hydroxyapatite crystals, resulting in cOC promoting bone mineral den-
sity [18], [19], [27]. COC is therefore found in the mineralized bone matrix, which is maintained by 
the bone formation, however, also bone resorption. Osteoclasts are cells responsible for bone re-
sorption by sticking to the surface of the bone. Osteoclasts attach to the surface by a microfilament-
rich site in the circumference, whilst the centrum of the area is known as the ruffled area. The ruf-
fled areas, also referred to as the resorption lacunae, hold a low pH of 4.5, which serves to dissolve 
minerals [28]. Moreover, proteolytic enzymes are released into the resorption lacuna to break down 
non-collagenous proteins, collagen-proteins, and matrix components [29]. This process includes the 
decarboxylation of the Gla-domains in cOC bound to the mineralized bone matrix resulting in ucOC 
[28]. UcOC has decreased affinity against Ca2+ ions in the bone matrix, which allows ucOC to enter 
the systemic circulation [30]. In the systemic circulation ucOC is thought to possess an endocrino-
logical role by regulating systemic levels of glucose, and the energy metabolism by binding to its 
respective specific receptors in certain organs[18], [30].  
The degree of carboxylation of OC determines the roles of action, such as cOC regulating minerali-
zation bone microenvironment, and ucOC regulating several physiological paths in distinct tissue. 
Figure 3.8 illustrates a simple overview of OCs path into the bone ECM as cOC, and into the sys-
temic circulation as ucOC. 
 
 
Figure 3.8. OCs VKD post-translational modification of Glu-residues and further path into the bone extracellular ma-
trix, osteoclasts, and systemic circulation (made in Biorender). 
3.2.2.1 The Structure of OC 
The primary structure of OC, in both H. sapiens & S. scrofa, including the signal peptide and pro-
peptide consists of 100 aa residues. The signal peptide sequence is found at the N-terminal at posi-
tion 1-23 in both species, and in H. sapiens the propeptide at 24-51[31], [32]. The database of Uni-
Prot does not have data concerning the length or location of the propeptide in S. scrofa, however the 
similarities found in the primary sequence of OC between H. sapiens & S. scrofa could lead to the 
assumption that the length and location of the propeptide would remain the same in the two species.

Focusing on the coding region for the mature protein the substitution of the hydrophobic Tyrosine 
(Y) in P02818 to the negatively charged Aspartic Acid (D) in F6PUF1 at position 54, removes an 
aromatic ring which removes possible π-interactions, and adds a negative charge, which can create 
new salt-bridge interactions. The substitution of the polar uncharged Glutamine (Q) in P02818 to 
the positively charged Histidine (H) in F6PUF1 at position 55, may introduce a local positive 
charge and new salt-bridge interactions. The substitution of the large hydrophobic aromatic Trypto-
phan (W) in P02818 to the small Glycine (G) in F6PUF1 at position 56 may result in loss of 𝜋-in-
teractions, and the small size of G may reduce hydrophobic packaging. The substitution of aromatic 
P in P02818 to hydrophobic Isoleucine (I) can stabilize H-bonding due to the restored H-bond do-
nor in the amino group and thus support α-helical or other secondary structures, in F6PUF1. Lastly, 
the substitution at position 100 of the two hydrophobic aas, V to A, might not alter the structure 
much, however as mentioned formerly A possesses a smaller size with a less branched side chain, 
which may lead to decreased hydrophobic packaging. In summary, there might be local alterations 
in the mature protein between the 2 species, although the overall structure might remain similar.

3.2.3 Unique cartilage matrix-associated protein (UCMA)  
UCMA is a secretory protein expressed in chondrocytes in the early phases of chondrogenesis[36]. 
Chondrogenesis is the process of mesenchymal stem cells differentiating into chondrocytes, which 
produces and maintains the cartilage[37]. UCMA facilitates inhibition of calcification in the vascu-
lar system, where it has been suggested that UCMA forms a complex together with MGP and an-
other molecule preventing calcification named fetuin-A. The UCMA-MGP-fetuin-A complex is 
found within extracellular vesicles (EV) released into the circulation by the VSMCs. The EVs act as 
a nucleation site for hydroxyapatite, in response to lack of active calcification inhibitors such as 
OC, or a high level of extracellular calcification[18]. Additionally, UCMA found within EVs re-
leased from macrophages has been thought to possess an anti-inflammatory role[18]. However, 
UCMA is newly discovered compared to MGP and OC, and therefore investigation must be done to 
define UCMAs exact function and expression pathway. 
 
3.2.3.1 The Structure of UCMA 
The primary structure of UCMA, in both H. sapiens & S. scrofa, including the signal peptide and 
propeptide consists of 138 aa residues. The signal peptide sequence is found at the N-terminal at po-
sition 1-26 in H. sapiens, however located at 1-27 in S. scrofa. The propeptide is found at 28-60 in 
H. sapiens, yet the database of UniProt does not specify the length or location of the propeptide in 
S. scrofa [38]. In similarity to MGP and OC, it is assumed that the location of the propeptide is the 
same in S. scrofa as in H. sapiens, due to their highly conserved composition. Additionally, it is as-
sumed that the signal peptide in H. sapiens comprises the aa residue at position 27, as S. scrofa has 
the aa residue at position 27 included in its signal peptide. The primary sequence with details con-
cerning amino acid composition and alignment of the UCMA in H. sapiens and S. scrofa is illus-
trated in Figure 3.13.

are non-identical at several positions.

3.3 Regulation of MGP, OC & UCMA  
Regulatory mechanisms are essential for cells to react on alterations in their environment, such as 
regulating expression of certain proteins, potentially being MGP, OC or UCMA, in terms of inhibit-
ing plaque formation. Regulation can occur at different phases of gene expression including tran-
scription, and the post-translational modification. This section will highlight some of the regulatory 
mechanisms regulating MGP, OC, and UCMA at the different phases. 
 
3.3.1 Signaling Pathways & Regulatory Mechanisms in The Transcription of MGP, 
OC & UCMA 
Regulatory mechanisms in the transcription comprise the act of epigenetic mechanisms or transcrip-
tion factors (TF) either activating or repressing the transcription of DNA, hence regulating the tran-
scription intensity of DNA into messenger ribonucleic acid (mRNA). In previous sections it was 
informed that MGP is expressed in VSMCs, ECs, and chondrocytes, OC is expressed in osteoblasts, 
and UCMA is expressed in chondrocytes. A common factor for MGP, OC and UCMA is that they 
are all expressed to some degree in cartilage and bone tissues, and potentially in soft tissues by 
VSMCs. However, in terms of atherosclerosis it is important to highlight the phenotypes of VSMCs 
altered by transdifferentiation, mentioned in 3.1.2 Progression of Atherosclerotic Lesions.  
VSMCs in the tunica media produces MGP, however in advanced atherosclerosis VSMCs transdif-
ferentiate into osteo-/chondrogenic phenotypes. Osteoblasts are mentioned in section 3.2.2 Oste-
ocalcin (OC) to produce OC, and chondrocytes are mentioned in section 3.2.3 Unique cartilage ma-
trix-associated protein (UCMA) to produce UCMA. This implies VSMCs ability to produce OC 
and UCMA, after being regulated by signals in the inflamed atherosclerotic lesions. These signal 
pathways regulating the phenotype of VSMC indirectly regulate the transcription of MGP, OC, and 
UCMA in the osteo-/chondrogenic VSMCs. The following subsections detail some of the regula-
tory TFs, which are activated in response to the transdifferentiation of VSMCs in atherosclerosis.  
 
3.3.1.1 Macrophage derived cytokines 
The understanding of VSMCs ability to transdifferentiate in a developing atherosclerotic plaque le-
sion is considered essential in terms of capturing which cytokines that influence the altered VSMC 
transcription to potentially express OC and UCMA. The entire inflammatory response to identifying 
oxidized LDLs infiltrated in the vessel wall leads to transdifferentiation and migration of VSMCs 
==PAGE:23==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
22 
from the tunica media. The following is a continuation of section 3.1.2Progression of Atherosclero-
tic Lesions, with respect to the details in the inflammatory signals. The dysfunctional ECs promote 
secretion of certain chemokines (e.g., monocyte chemoattractant protein-1 (MCP-1)) secreted by 
dysfunctional ECs, macrophages, and monocytes in the tunica intima. The recruitment and migra-
tion of monocytes into the tunica intima at the atherosclerotic lesion is executed in response to the 
mentioned chemokines[1]. Monocytes differentiate into macrophages in the tunica intima, where 
they secrete cytokines as pro-inflammatory signals. Some of these macrophage derived cytokines, 
which are considered to stimulate VSMCs into transdifferentiation are tumor necrosis factor-α 
(TNF-𝛼), and interleukin 6 (IL-6), amongst other[1], [11]. These cytokines are thought to stimulate 
VSMCs to transdifferentiate into the osteo-/chondrogenic phenotype. The osteo-/chondrogenic phe-
notype of VSMC is thought to express various transcription factors as e.g., RUNX2, SOX9, 
amongst other, which potentially promote the expression of OC and UCMA [10].  
 
3.3.2 Regulatory Mechanisms in The Post-translational Modifications of MGP, OC 
& UCMA 
Regulation mechanisms in the post-translational modification could influence the modifications of 
the proteins post to being translated from mRNA to protein. The modifications could determine the 
protein activity, protein stability and alter the interactions of the final protein. Common for all Gla-
proteins, including MGP, OC, and UCMA, is their requirement of VK as one of the cofactors in the 
conversion of Glu-residues into Gla-domains by 𝛾-carboxylation in the post-translational modifica-
tion, which reflects Gla-proteins dependency on VK to get properly activated.  
 
3.3.2.1 VKD 𝛾-carboxylation & The VK Cycle  
The VKD γ-carboxylation performed by γ-carboxy glutamyl carboxylase (GGCX) takes place in 
the ER and is essential to convert Glu-residues to Gla-residues. This modification will increase the 
negative charge of final protein, which in case of e.g., MGP will increase its affinity towards Ca2+-
ions. Aforementioned, depicts VK as an important resource in the body, therefore an essential pro-
cess called the VK cycle ensures recycling of VK to prevent and maintain levels of VK in the body, 
which is challenged by high excretion rates and small reservoirs of VK in the body[43]. The VK cy-
cle is illustrated in 
Figure 3.17.  
==PAGE:24==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
23 
 
 
Figure 3.17. Vitamin K Cycle and its Role in Gamma-Carboxylation [12]. (Made with Marvin Demo sketch). 
 
As illustrated in 
Figure 3.17, VK (green) is reduced to VK hydroquinone (Fejl! Henvisningskilde ikke fundet.) by 
VK epoxide reductase complex, subunit 1 (VKORC1). VKH2 (orange), O! and CO! are used as co-
factors in the enzymatic reaction performed by GGCX ensuring γ-carboxylation of the Glu residues 
into Gla residues. During this reaction, VKH₂ (orange) is oxidized to form VK epoxide (pink), 
which is subsequently reduced back to VK (green) by VKORC1, allowing the cycle to repeat. The 
blue arrows illustrate reduction reactions, while the red arrow represents the oxidation reaction.  
 
3.3.2.2 Vitamin K  
VK was discovered by the Danish biochemist named Henrik Carl Peter Dam, in the years between 
1928-30. Henrik Dam investigated in chicks fed with diet free from sterols (including cholesterol) 
which at that time were previously shown in other studies to result in delayed coagulation and hem-
orrhages under the skin. Despite giving the chicks, a diet including cholesterol, and several other 
vitamins the bleeding tendency did not take hold. Finally, a diet comprising green leaves and hog 
liver were shown to prevent the bleeding symptoms. The experiments indicated the importance in 
the coagulation system of the previously unidentified fat-soluble factor, which was given the name 
“Vitamin K”. The letter “K” was used due to the Danish word for coagulation – “Koagulation” [44]. 
Today, VK is known as an essential co-factor in the VK cycle, which is essential for 𝛾-carboxyla-
tion to take place. Gla-domains are Glu-residues with an additional carboxyl-group, which increases 
the overall negative charge of the residue. The increased negative charge of the residue increases 
the activity of MGP, OC, and UCMA and other VKDPs at various sites in the body. The subsection 
3.3.2.1 The VK Cycle elaborates on the mechanisms recycling VK, whilst 𝛾-carboxylation of Glu-
residues occurs in the ER.

VK is a fat-soluble vitamin, which exists in 2 forms: VK1 and VK2, seen in Figure 3.18. Both forms 
are of natural origin, however VK1 is a phylloquinone of plant origin, whereas VK2 covers over a 
series of compounds of menaquinones, which mainly are produced by certain bacterial strains in-
cluding some certain cultures in the human intestine [15]. It is found in literature that VK1 is pre-
dominantly taken up by the liver, whilst VK2 is more prone to accumulate in arteries or other extra-
hepatic areas (locations outside the liver), which may affect different activation of different VKDP 
[45].  
 
 
 
Figure 3.18. Chemical structures of the two forms of VK. In the top VK1 is illustrated, and in the bottom VK2 is presented (made in 
Chemsketch).  
 
VK1 and VK2 share a 2-methyl-1,4-naphthoquinone ring and a side chain starting at position 3 in the 
ring. The side chain begins with an isoprenoid unit in both VK1 and VK2, however the side chain be-
yond this point: VK1 has a saturated aliphatic phytyl chain, whereas VK2 has an unsaturated ali-
phatic side chain of repeating isoprene residues, each with a double bond. The number of isoprene 
units in VK2’s side chain varies depending on the specific menaquinone (MK-n) subtype and the 
number of isoprene units is denoted with an ”n” [46][47], [48]  
3.4 The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
Translational studies serve as a steppingstone in the process going from research to clinical studies 
in humans. An ideal translational study aims to translate results from animal studies, in which dis-
coveries found in research are tested to be able to plan clinical studies with humans as test objects 
[49]. Furthermore, animal models are applied for basic research purposes, such as investigating 
mechanisms and to identify drug targets. 
3.4.1 Animal Studies  
Animal studies cover a broad range of different species including rats, mice, rabbits, pigs, etc., 
which all serve different purposes. As an example, the mouse model is the most common animal 
model applied in EU for scientific purposes in EU. In 2019, 5,459,433 mice were used in research 
studies conducted in EU [50]. The mouse model excels in being a low-cost model where mice are 
easy to breed with rapid gestation times, short lifespans, and a fully sequenced genetic map [50]. 
However, large animal studies with pigs provide with aspects of size, genetics and physiology that 
mimics humans to a larger extent than mice [51]. In addition, pigs serve as optimal models for 
Vitamin K1: 
Vitamin K2: 
==PAGE:26==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
25 
particularly imaging, tissue engineering, and surgical procedures, as their size and organ dimensions 
closely mimic those of humans [52]. Large animal studies with pigs, including miniature pigs, are 
being more used in research than before with 82,819 pigs used in EU in 2019 [51]. Regulatory bod-
ies require test results from studies performed with two distinct species for a new drug to be ap-
proved for testing in humans. Utilizing pigs in large animal studies can ideally bridge the gap be-
tween studies made with rodents and humans. However, large animal models including farm pigs 
are expensive due to their size which requires more space, food, and eventually drugs. In compari-
son to larger farm pigs, the miniature pigs have their advantages in their smaller size, which makes 
them easier to handle, cheaper to maintain due to less requirements of food, space, and drugs. Vari-
ous breeds of minipigs are used in animal studies as e.g., Göttingen, and Ossabaw miniature pigs 
[49]. 
3.4.2 The Advantages of utilizing The Obese Ossabaw Miniature Pig Model in Ath-
erosclerosis Studies 
The Ossabaw minipig serves as an exemplary translational preclinical miniature pig model for in-
vestigating human cardiovascular and metabolic diseases. The underlying reason for the aforemen-
tioned is that Ossabaw minipigs possess larger anatomical dimensions, which are more representa-
tive of human physiology compared to smaller miniature pigs, such as the Göttingen minipig. The 
larger dimensions in Ossabaw minipigs are particularly relevant in studies involving coronary vas-
culature, as smaller models often have conduit coronary arteries that are too narrow for testing de-
vices like imaging catheters and stents.  
In the context of metabolic disease research, the Ossabaw minipig has been widely used to model 
human obesity and MetS. MetS is defined as a condition that encompasses multiple cardiovascular 
disease risk factors, as for example obesity, high blood pressure, atypical concentrations of lipids in 
the circulation, and unusual glucose metabolism [53]. Studies have shown that Ossabaw minipigs as 
response to increasing in weight whilst being placed on a MetS diet, developed glucose intolerance, 
insulin resistance, increased plasma triglycerides, hypertension, coronary vascular pathologies in-
cluding coronary atheroma, diffuse atherosclerosis, calcification, and in-stent stenosis. Furthermore, 
if the MetS diet is supplemented with >1% cholesterol then the Ossabaw minipig model will relia-
bly increase in plasma LDL [49]. All the complications as results of a MetS diet together with obe-
sity show the great correlation that exists between Ossabaw minipigs and human pathogenesis, 
however some of the developed complications are not consistently equivalent to humans.  
Additionally, studies have found that vasoconstriction of mesenteric arteries was more pronounced 
in Ossabaw minipigs than in Göttingen minipigs, while vasodilation was less pronounced, suggest-
ing a predisposition towards atherosclerosis.  
The anatomical and physiological similarities between the Ossabaw minipig and humans, along 
with its well-documented use in modeling conditions such as obesity, MetS, and atherosclerosis, 
makes it a highly relevant model for biomedical research. Findings from studies like those of the 
study ( (Eickelmann, Lieder, Sturek, Heusch, & Kleinbongard, 2024), [54]) reinforces the Ossabaw 
minipig’s utility in bridging preclinical research with human clinical outcomes, particularly in test-
ing coronary devices, studying vascular dysfunction and metabolic diseases.

Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model

4 Project Aim 
This master thesis aims to investigate the regulation of the VKD Gla-proteins; MGP, OC, and 
UCMA in response to obesity, utilizing tissue samples from the translational Ossabaw miniature pig 
model, comprising both obese and lean Ossabaw miniature pigs as references.  
The obese Ossabaw miniature pig model is applied, since obesity serves as a risk factor of develop-
ing atherosclerosis, and the model serves to mimic human pathogenesis to a large extent. MGP, OC, 
and UCMA are thought to possess a role in atherosclerosis development; therefore, both obese and 
lean tissue samples were examined to identify potential regulatory changes in the expression of 
these proteins that may relate to atherosclerosis. To accomplish the project aim, the following ex-
perimental activities were conducted.  
Primer pairs were designed to quantify the amount of RNA from MGP, OC, and UCMA in heart, 
SAT, PAX, liver, and aorta tissue samples by performing RT-qPCR assays. RNA samples were ex-
tracted from the tissue samples. Then cDNA synthesis of the RNA samples was performed, and 
then RT-qPCR assays were conducted to determine the RQ of MGP, OC and UCMA in the respec-
tive samples. The RQ was used to gain insight into potential regulation in gene expression of the 
target genes. In addition, an ELISA targeting the OC protein in plasma samples and SAT, heart and 
liver tissue extracts was conducted. The ELISA was completed to determine the concentration of 
OC protein in the respective tissue as response to obesity, a risk factor for atherosclerotic develop-
ment. The quantification of the systemic protein levels of OC in plasma samples was investigated to 
disclose OCs potential role as a biomarker for early signs of atherosclerosis development as result 
of obesity. In the following sections, the methods and results are described, explained, analyzed, 
and discussed in terms of investigating the regulation of gene and protein expression of MGP, OC, 
and UCMA in relation to potential development of atherosclerosis in obese Ossabaw miniature pigs.

5 Theoretical Background & Experimental Strategy 
The theoretical background of the experiments applied in this thesis study is further described in the 
following subsections. Figure 5.1 provides an overview of the experimental procedure including all 
the activities performed in the laboratory at the Innate Immunology & Metabolism research group 
during this master thesis. 
 
 
Figure 5.1. Overview of the Experimental Workflow. 
The process initiates at the upper left box and proceeds with the outlined arrows between each box. (made in BioRen-
der). 
5.1 The Ossabaw Obesity Model & Tissue Selection 
In this master thesis, tissue samples from 4 lean control and 3 obese Ossabaw miniature pigs were 
selected to be investigated in terms of finding a potential altered regulation of MGP, OC, and 
UCMA due to obesity related metabolic differences. The pigs used for this study were all castrated 
males at an age between 6-7 months. Prior to the conduction of this master thesis, the pigs went 
through an experimental setup where they consumed two different diets for 11 weeks. The lean pigs 
were fed with a maximum of 900g/day with a diet of 3.3 kcal/g consisting of 74.6% carbohydrates, 
18.6% protein, 6.8% fat (origin from sunflower or soybean oils), and 4.1% fructose (or other sug-
ars). The obese pigs were fed with a maximum 1kg/day with a diet of 4.6 kcal/g consisting of 41% 
carbohydrates, 11% protein, 48% fat (origin from lard & soybean oil), 20.5% fructose, and 0.5% 
cholesterol. Tissue samples were collected prior to this master thesis. After 11 weeks, the tissue 
samples were collected. The pigs were individually put under sedation, and firstly a blood sample 
was collected. After the blood sampling the pigs were put down by injecting pentobarbital into their 
heart. Subsequently, heart tissue, abdominal SAT, liver tissue, and aorta tissue amongst other tissue 
==PAGE:29==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
28 
were selected and cut into small pieces, sampled in labelled tubes with RNAlater and frozen in liq-
uid nitrogen, and stored at -20℃. 
The selection of relevant tissue comprised SAT, heart tissue, PAX, liver tissue, and aorta tissue 
samples for RQ of target DNA. Furthermore, the aforementioned tissue samples and serum were 
utilized to quantify the OC protein expression in the respective tissue. 
5.1.1 Subcutaneous Fat Tissue  
Obesity and accumulation of fat tissue is a risk factor for developing atherosclerosis; however, the 
Gla-proteins might be present in the adipose tissue. Studies in literature assumes that MGP within 
SAT possess a role in adipogenesis [55]. Furthermore, other studies have suggested OC’s potential 
role in increasing the secretion of adiponectin from adipose tissue, which potentially could increase 
insulin sensitivity [30]. Aforementioned, shaped foundation for examining how obesity could affect 
the RQ of MGP, OC and UCMA in SAT, and thereby the regulation of expression of the respective 
genes. This was done by conducting RT-qPCR assays with relevant primers targeting MGP, OC 
and UCMA. In addition, studies in literature have found that VK has been identified in large con-
centrations within adipose tissue, which could give rise to VK insufficiency in obese individuals. 
As mentioned earlier Gla proteins are VKD, and lack of VK could affect the availability of VK for 
the occurrence of VKD 𝛾-carboxylation of Gla proteins in other tissues at other locations[56]. An 
ELISA targeting OC protein in SAT protein extracts could give insights in how the protein quantity 
of OC might differ between lean and obese SAT. However, the ELISA does not differentiate be-
tween the degree of carboxylation of the OC proteins.  
5.1.2 Heart Tissue  
The heart is the vascular system’s main organ, due to its role of ensuring a consistent blood flow 
providing nutrients and oxygen to cells throughout the entire body. It previously mentioned how the 
blood vessel’s tunic layers might be affected by obesity and inflammation. The heart tissue might be 
affected by the consequences of obesity since the heart organ consists of muscle and blood vessels 
[57]. The RQ of MGP, OC, and UCMA in obese heart tissue is investigated by RT-qPCR to gain 
insight in how obesity regulates the gene expression as cardiac response to possible metabolic in-
flammation and formation of early-stage atherosclerosis caused by obesity. In addition, conducting 
an ELISA targeting OC in heart tissue extracts could give insight in the protein quantity of OC, 
thereby measuring the possible change in OC protein levels in response to obesity.  
5.1.3 PAX Stabilized Blood Samples  
The investigation of the systemic gene expression of MGP, OC, and UCMA was performed utiliz-
ing cDNA samples created from PAX stabilized blood samples as templates in RT-qPCR. The 
PAXgene RNA Tubes lyses the cells in the blood sample, isolates and purifies RNA in terms of 
protecting the RNA molecules against RNase degradation. Aforementioned, ensures that intracellu-
lar RNA from whole blood samples is represented in the created cDNA samples applied in RT-
qPCR [58]. The PAX samples mainly comprise RNA expressed in the systemic white blood cells, 
since mature red blood cells and platelets do not contain any nuclei. However, it is possible that im-
mature red blood cells contain globin mRNA expressed in prior precursor phases towards maturing. 
The composition in PAX samples is therefore both comprising RNA expressed by circulating white 
==PAGE:30==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
29 
blood cells, and globin RNA from immature red blood cells, where last-mentioned could potentially 
decrease the sensitivity in detecting target genes in RT-qPCR [59].  
5.1.4 Liver Tissue Samples  
The liver regulates the lipid, glucose, and protein metabolism. The liver synthesizes and degrades 
lipids, stores and releases glucose and synthesizes VKDPs comprised in the coagulation cascade, 
amongst other plasma proteins. Furthermore, the liver produces bile, which supports uptake of VK 
amongst other nutrients in the intestine and can store VK amongst other vitamins [60]. However, 
the liver is affected by obesity and can in some cases develop to become a fatty liver, which could 
influence its metabolic activity. The RQ of MGP, OC and UCMA in liver tissue is examined by 
RT-qPCR to get insight in how obesity possibly affects the liver cells to up- or downregulate gene 
expression of the MGP, OC, and UCMA genes. In addition, conducting an ELISA targeting OC in 
protein extracts from liver tissue could give insight in the protein quantity of OC, thereby measuring 
the possible change in OC protein levels in response to obesity, since this may indicate early pro-
gression of atherosclerosis.  
5.1.5 Aorta Tissue Samples  
Aorta is the largest blood vessel in the body. In aforementioned sections it has been informed that 
VSMCs in the blood vessel walls are able to express MGP and OC to a certain degree. The VSMC 
gene expression of the mentioned proteins depends on external factors affecting the phenotype of 
the VSMCs. Performance of RT-qPCR with cDNA samples created from aorta RNA will poten-
tially provide information of how obesity likely affects the regulation of MGP, OC and UCMA ex-
pression in aorta.  
5.1.6 Plasma Samples  
Plasma is the clear liquid upper phase of an anti-coagulant treated whole blood sample, where the 
cells in the sample have settled to the bottom of the sampling tube. This means that plasma samples 
comprise all proteins and blood clotting factors, however not the cells from the blood sample [61]. 
Therefore, plasma is advantageous to use in immunoassays quantifying the systemic protein levels 
of OC. 
5.2 Relative Quantification of MGP, OC & UCMA by RT-qPCR  
RT-qPCR is a quantitative assay for RNA in which RNA is reverse transcribed into a complemen-
tary DNA (cDNA) strand, which is amplified by PCR and quantified by measuring the rate of for-
mation of double stranded PCR products, referred to as amplicons, reflecting the quantity of a spe-
cific RNA transcript in the respective tissue. The RNA transcripts, and thereby the generated 
dsDNA in the RT-qPCR, is correlated with the relative quantification of the corresponding protein 
expressed in the given tissue. Prior to RT-qPCR, the designing of relevant primers, extraction of 
RNA from the selected tissue mentioned previously, measurement of quantity and quality of the ex-
tracted RNA followed by a cDNA synthesis of the extracted RNA must be conducted. The follow-
ing subsections elaborates upon each of the procedures, and lastly the RT-qPCR assay. 
==PAGE:31==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
30 
5.2.1 Primer Design 
Designing relevant primer pairs matching the complementary genetic sequence representing the de-
sired protein of interest is an essential part of a successful RT-qPCR assay.  
 
The gene-specific primers used in the RT-qPCR experiment were designed to follow the guideline 
in Appendix 3. 
Initially the splice-site variants of each protein are identified to design primer pairs amplifying all or 
selected splice-site variants of interest. Splice-site variants are variants of the same genetic se-
quence, which differ in their exact location of the splice site between exons and introns. This altera-
tion leads to some exons being excluded or some introns being included in the RNA sequence cod-
ing for the protein. It is worth mentioning that the number of splice variants differ from one specie 
to another, and potentially there might exist unidentified splice variants in the Ossabaw genome, 
which may interfere with the efficiency of the primer pairs. The primers in this experiment are 
mainly designed as over introns (oi-primer) -primers and have further been designed to comply 
with the following: GC rich in the 3’ end, a length of 18-22 bases, and position as close to the 3’ 
end of the target DNA as possible.  
Oi-primer designs diminish the chances of false positives by reducing the chance of primers binding 
to contaminating genomic DNA. Introns are included in genomic DNA; however, introns are not 
found in cDNA. An oi-primer spanning over an exon-exon junction can therefore not bind to the 
template of a contaminating genomic DNA, since it comprises introns [62]. Therefore, it should be 
taken into consideration when analyzing the results that i-primer pairs possess an increased risk of 
binding to contaminating genomic DNA giving rise to PCR products that do not reflect RNA abun-
dance. 
Designing primers with a GC rich 3’end will increase the stability of the primers, due to the 
stronger hydrogen bonding, however, to avoid primer-dimer formation too many repeated G or C 
bases should be avoided [63]. Primer-dimer formation is the result of unspecific binding of primers. 
The length of a primer will influence the efficiency of the primer’s ability to bind to their target 
DNA sequence. Furthermore, the melting temperature (Tm) is also reliant on the primer length, 
which further emphasizes the importance of designing short primers. The amount of G and C in the 
primers will also increase the Tm, due to the extra hydrogen bond in comparison to A and T bases 
[63]. 
The majority of reverse transcriptase protocols apply oligo deoxythymidine primers (oligo-dT pri-
mers) comprising a segment dT, which anneals to the poly-A tail of the mRNA, and function as 
foundation for the reverse transcriptase to bind and synthesize DNA. This means that the 3’ of the 
DNA in general has a greater chance of being reverse transcribed, wherefore it is more beneficial to 
select primers close to the 3’ end of DNA.  
 
5.2.2 Extraction of RNA & cDNA Synthesis  
Prior to performing the synthesis of cDNA, which is used as a template in the RT-qPCR, RNA is 
extracted from the selected tissue. The quantity and purity of the extracted RNA samples are deter-
mined by measuring and analyzing the optical density (OD) at different absorbances by using a 
==PAGE:32==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
31 
NanoDrop spectrophotometer. The extracted RNA’s quantity can be measured at A260, and the pu-
rity of the RNA samples are determined by measuring the ratio between A260/A280 and 
A260/A230. The ratio between A260/A230 measures the degree of contaminating organic com-
pounds (e.g., ethanol), and the ratio between A260/280 determined the degree of potential contami-
nating proteins in the RNA samples. The quantity and purity will affect downstream reactions, as 
for example low quantity of RNA would result in low amount of cDNA leading to lack of signals in 
the following RT-qPCR. Furthermore, poor purity could mean that the RNA is degraded by en-
zymes in the sample, which possibly could result in downstream inconclusive RT-qPCR results.  
 
The RNA quality (integrity) is measured by using the Bioanalyzer technique, which determines the 
RNA integrity number (RIN) for each RNA sample. The Bioanalyzer is conducted by a gel-based 
on-chip electrophoresis which separates the negatively charged RNA molecules by size by utilizing 
a voltage gradient. The detector detects the RNA molecules at different time points, since larger 
RNA molecules move slower in the gel matrix than the small RNA molecules. The labeled RNA is 
measured by laser-induced fluorescence, and the results are given as electropherograms which are 
then analyzed by the Bioanalyzer, which determines the RIN of each RNA sample. The RIN soft-
ware algorithm will classify each RNA sample in accordance to how intact or degraded the total 
RNA sample is. The RIN is given as a number between 1 and 10, where 1 represents the most de-
graded, and 10 represents the most intact level of RNA. A high RIN is desired to be able to gain re-
sults where primers bind efficiently to intact entire fragments of RNA, reflecting a more accurate 
biological result, which is not affected by degradation and technical noise, when running the RT-
qPCR.  
5.2.3 cDNA Synthesis  
The conversion of RNA to cDNA is performed by the enzyme reverse transcriptase (RT) together 
with deoxynucleotide triphosphates (dNTPs). Synthesis of RNA to cDNA is essential for amplifica-
tion in RT-qPCR to take place, since the DNA polymerase used for the PCR can solely bind to 
DNA, and not RNA[64]. cDNA functions as a template for primers to anneal followed by DNA pol-
ymerase extending the corresponding strand.  
5.2.4 RT-qPCR Assay 
The RT-qPCR assay initiates with a hot start to activate a DNA polymerase, which is then followed 
by a certain number of PCR cycles comprising denaturation of dsDNA and amplification of the tar-
get DNA. The amplification covers both the annealing of primers to the complementary DNA re-
gion, and extension performed by DNA polymerase. DNA polymerase recognizes the free 3’-OH 
group on the primers annealed to their complementary sequence and initiates extension of new 
dsDNA by adding dNTPs to the 3’-end, the product of this is referred to as an amplicon. The re-
porter dye in this master thesis is a fluorescent dye which detects dsDNA between each cycle, and 
the fluorescence will therefore be proportional to the number of amplicons, which is then quantified 
in Real-Time. The detection of the increasing accumulation of amplicons per cycle is affected by 
the number of cDNA molecules corresponding to the gene in question at the start of the reaction, 
which reflects the amount of expressed RNA present in the selected tissue.  Figure 5.2 illustrates the 
phases within one cycle of RT-qPCR.  
==PAGE:33==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
32 
 
Figure 5.2. Overview of the phases in one cycle of RT-qPCR relative to the temperature illustrated on the y-axis, while 
the x-axis represents time points during the reaction (arbitrary units). (Made in BioRender). 
The RT-qPCR generates data concerning the number of quantification cycles (Cq). The Cq value is 
the certain number of cycles that is needed for the PCR reactions fluorescent dye to create enough 
signal to be able to pass the predetermined threshold. A low Cq value is a result a high amount of 
the target genetic sequence providing high fluorescent signals in the early cycles. A high Cq value 
indicates a low amount of the target genetic sequence in the cDNA sample tested, since an in-
creased amount of amplification cycles are required for sufficient signal to pass the threshold. Fig-
ure 5.3, illustrates an example of a quantitative amplification curve, and the phases generated during 
RT-qPCR. 
 
Figure 5.3. Quantitative Amplification Curve.  
The x-axis represents the number of amplification cycles, and the y-axis represents the measured fluorescence. Three 
phases are present during an RT-qPCR. 1. Initial Lag phase, 2. Exponential phase, and 3. Plateau Phase, which are all 
illustrated on the graph at the phases when they appear. In addition, the Cq value of this example is highlighted in blue. 
(made in BioRender). 
==PAGE:34==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
33 
In the quantitative amplification curve is illustrating as a sigmoidal curve with an S-shape. This 
means that the curves’ 3 phases consist of an initial flat lag phase, in this phase no signal is de-
tected. The second phase is the exponential phase, where the amount of PCR product, and thereby 
the detection signal, will double for every cycle. At some point the decreasing number of available 
reagents will become limited and the exponential growth stops, and a plateau is reached as the third 
phase. The normalized fluorescence along the y-axis is normalized to remove technical noise which 
is not reflecting the amplification process.  
 
Following the quantitative amplification, then the melt phase is initiated. The melt phase creates a 
melt curve, which enables determination of the specificity of the primer pairs used. The melt curve 
defines the product Tm where one-half of the double-stranded PCR products had denatured to single 
stranded DNA. The product Tm depends on the length and the composition of bases in the PCR 
product. The product Tm for one PCR product created by a certain primer pair should therefore be 
consistent across all cDNA samples tested, which would form one distinct peak. If several peaks 
appear on the melt curve, then it is a sign that non-specific products were created during the PCR, 
which could be a result of poor primer specificity or self-annealing primers (primer-dimers). Non-
specific data is removed from the data since this would interfere with the specific data.  
 
The corresponding Cq data to the specific melt data can be used to calculate the RQ of the gene of 
interest in selected cDNA samples. In terms of being able to determine the RQ, then a dilution se-
ries comprising a pool of the tested cDNA samples, must be included in the RT-qPCR run. The data 
of the dilution series will represent all cDNA samples and is used to create a standard curve. The 
amplification in RT-qPCR follows an exponential regression until the plateau phase is reached, as 
seen in Equation 5.1, where the PCR product doubles for every PCR cycle.

The linearity of the trendline obtained from the standard curve is evaluated in terms of using the 
trendline’s slope to determine the PCR efficiency. A high linearity is the result of Cq-values con-
sistently increasing in correlation with decreasing concentrations. A valid correlation coefficient 
(R2) of the trendline holds a value of R2 > 0.99 [66]. 
The slope of the trendline is utilized to determine the PCR efficiency, which is a result of how effi-
cient the target gene is amplified during the RT-qPCR. The amplification in an RT-qPCR increases 
in an exponential manner, and a consistent doubling of amplicons gives rise to an ideal PCR effi-
ciency of 100% [67].

log (2)  
Equation 5.8 
All the efficiency corrected Cq-values are normalized, by subtracting the mean of the efficiency 
corrected Cq-values of the reference genes applied, as shown below in Equation 5.9. 
 
∆Cq = Cq788 −Mean Ref Cq788 
Equation 5.9 
Where, 𝚫𝐂𝐪 is the normalized Cq788 , 𝐂𝐪𝐞𝐟𝐟 is the efficiency corrected Cq value for the given sam-
ple, and Mean Ref 𝐂𝐪𝐞𝐟𝐟 is the mean value of the Cq788 obtained from the reference genes.  
Data normalization of the efficiency corrected Cq-values is done in terms of removing technical 
variations from the data. Therefore, it is essential to include reference genes with stable expression 
in all samples, since the reference genes are affected by the exact same parameters as the target 
genes. Subtracting the references’ Cqeff  from the samples’ Cqeff  would lead to the only difference 
==PAGE:37==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
36 
being biological up- and downregulation of the genes.  
The relative quantities of the PCR product are found by converting the data to linear scale by rais-
ing 2, reflecting the perfect doubling per efficiency corrected Cq value, to the power of the differ-
ence between the normalized and efficiency corrected samples holding the highest efficiency cor-
rected and normalized Cq value aΔCq;<3;7=>c and the remaining samples’ Cq values aΔCq=?6@17c. 
This is the same as calculating the fold change between the aforementioned, which can be seen in 
the following Equation 5.10. 
 
RQ = 2A$%%&'%#()
2∆$%(*+,-# = 2CA$%%&'%#()5A$%(*+,-#D 
Equation 5.10 
The sample with the highest Cq value is assumingly the sample with the lowest expression of target 
DNA and is selected as the baseline for relative quantification. The fold change is applied in terms 
of determining each sample’s RQ of target DNA set relative to the baseline sample comprising the 
highest Cq value.  
 
In this thesis, RT-qPCR is applied to determine the RQ of MGP, OC and UCMA in tissue samples 
of heart, PAX, SAT, liver, and aorta from Ossabaw miniature pigs. This was done to examine the 
possible difference in regulation of the respective genes in the aforementioned tissue in both obese 
and lean pigs.  
 
5.3 Enzyme-Linked Immunosorbent Assay (ELISA) 
ELISA is used to quantify and detect a specific epitope of a target protein in a sample of interest. 
The ELISA method immobilizes the protein of interest and quantifies by an enzymatic reaction by 
e.g., peroxidase (POD). ELISA can be performed as either direct ELISA, indirect ELISA, or as 
sandwich ELISA. In both direct and indirect ELISA, the protein of interest is immobilized to the 
wells of a microtiter plate. However, in direct ELISA an enzyme-linked primary antibody binds to 
the epitope of the protein of interest; whereas in indirect ELISA a primary antibody binds to the 
epitope and a secondary enzyme-linked antibody have affinity and binds to the primary antibody. 
The sandwich ELISA is carried out with a plate coated with capture antibodies with affinity to the 
epitope of the protein, and an enzyme-linked detection antibody with affinity to another epitope, on 
the now, immobilized protein, which results in the antibodies forming a sandwich with the target 
protein in the middle.  
In this master thesis, a commercial sandwich ELISA kit was utilized to identify and quantify OC of 
porcine origin in plasma samples, and in tissue extracts of SAT, liver, and heart. The kit comprised 
a plate coated with solid-phase monoclonal capture antibodies binding to a specific epitope of OC, 
which is not disclosed by the supplier. In order to quantify the amount of capture antibody bound to 
OC, then biotinylated detection antibodies bind to another epitope of OC. A horse radish peroxide 
(HRP)-Streptavidin conjugate binds to the biotinylated detection antibodies, and the HRP part of 
the conjugate will create a colored soluble by an enzymatic reaction using a 3,3′,5,5′-Tetra-
methylbenzidine (TMB) and H2O2 containing substrate solution. An acidic stop solution is added to 
==PAGE:38==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
37 
stop the enzymatic reaction, which will change the blue colored product to yellow. The intensity of 
yellow color in each well is proportional to the captured amount of OC. Finally, the quantification is 
done by measuring the A450 of the wells and create a standard curve of the standard series in order 
to determine the OC concentration of the remaining samples utilizing the equation of the standard 
curve’s trendline [68]. The steps in ELISA are illustrated in Figure 5.4. 
 
Figure 5.4. Sandwich ELISA with affinity to OC (made in BioRender). 
 
 
==PAGE:39==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model
