6.2 Methods 
6.2.1 RT-qPCR  
6.2.1.1 Primer Design 
The primer pairs in this study were designed with respect to the information given in section 5.2.1 
Primer Design with foundation in the guide found in Appendix 3. The Ensembl Genome Browser 
was used to look up the splice-site variants of MGP, OC, and UCMA in the genome of Ossabaw 
Miniature pigs. It was found that MGP had 2 identified splice site variants, holding the UniProt en-
tries: Q8MJ39 and A0A4X1V4A6. Solely, 1 splice variant of OC was identified as F6PUF1. Fur-
thermore, 2 splice variants of UCMA were identified as A0A8D1N4R8 & A0A287A5H4. The pri-
mer pairs were designed to fit identical regions to enhance specificity across splice variants.  
The design of the primer pairs favored primers spanning an exon-exon junction resulting in oi-de-
signs to mitigate the risk of amplifying genomic DNA. The Primer 3 browser was applied to test 
various forward primer designs in terms of meeting the parameters mentioned in Appendix 3. In re-
turn, Primer3 suggested a reverse primer design, which also aligned with the aforementioned pa-
rameters including the following: A product size between 80-120 base pairs (bp) and a maximum 
melting temperature between 59.0℃ – 61.0℃, whilst applying the Breslauer et al. 1986 thermody-
namics settings in Primer3.  
Overall, 2 oi primer pairs (MGP 229 & 230) were designed for targeting MGP DNA, 3 oi and 2 i 
primer pairs (OC 232, 235 & 237; OC 231 & OC 236) were made to target OC DNA, and 2 oi pri-
mer pairs (UCMA 233 & 234) were designed to target UCMA DNA. The reference primer pairs 
targeted YWHAZ and PPIA because of their common use as housekeeping genes in eukaryotic cells 
across various tissue. The reference primer pairs were designed prior to this master thesis. The ref-
erences were expected to provide steady gene expression levels to be used for normalization of the 
RT-qPCR results. All primer pairs, including information concerning length, GC% ratio, and se-
quence are all listed in Table 6.3. 
All raw output from Primer3 concerning the primer pairs is found in Appendix 4 Appendix . As 
mentioned earlier, the primer designs were created to fit into all identified splice-site variants, 
which required an overview of the individual splice-site variants’ exon/intron composition to locate 
the primer pairs’ binding site at similar locations in each splice variant.  
To illustrate how each design was made, then Figure 6.1 and Figure 6.2 are presented below as an 
example. The 2 identified splice variants of MGP (UniProt entry: Q8MJ39) and (UniProt entry: 
A0A4X1V4A6), differs in the beginning of the genomic sequence. The MGP 229 primer pair had to 
be designed to match the sequence after introns 2-3 in Q8MJ39 and after introns 3-4 in 
A0A4X1V4A6. The same rule applied for the design of the MGP 230 primer pair. An overview of 
all the identified splice variants from Ensembl, their exons/introns composition and the location of 
the primer pairs binding site is found in Appendix 5. 
 
==PAGE:42==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
41 
 
Figure 6.1. Exons/introns composition of MGP (UniProt Entry: Q8MJ39), and the primer pair MGP (229) binding 
sites. The dark green fields are untranslated regions (UTR).  
 
Figure 6.2. Exons/introns composition of MGP, splice site variant, (UniProt Entry: A0A4X1V4A6), and the primer pair 
MGP (229) binding sites. The dark green fields are untranslated regions (UTR). 
6.2.1.2 RNA Extraction 
The RNA extraction of the PAX, SAT and some of the liver tissue samples were performed prior to 
this master thesis study by other parts. However, RNA extraction of heart, aorta, and remaining 
liver tissue samples were performed during this master study. The RNA extraction methods are de-
scribed in the following subsections, whilst the kits and materials used for RNA extraction are men-
tioned in Table 12.1. 
 
==PAGE:43==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
42 
6.2.1.2.1 RNA Extraction from Tissue 
The RNA extraction from the tissue samples, mentioned in Table 6.1, was performed by utilizing 
the miRNeasy Mini Kit (Qiagen #217004), the RNase-Free DNase set (Qiagen #79254), the ho-
mogenizer GentleMACS, and M tubes. The protocol can be found in Appendix 6. The contents of 
the kits are presented in Table 12.1 found in Appendix 2. 
At first, the centrifuge was placed at 4℃ followed by the working area being sanitized with 0.5% 
NaOH. Then, 1 mL of QIAzol Lysis Reagent was pipetted into a number of separate M tubes, corre-
sponding to the number of tissue samples undergoing RNA extraction. QIAzol Lysis Reagent was 
used to support the lysis of cells in the tissue and degradation of proteins. Thereafter, a scalpel was 
used to cut approximately 30 mg of each tissue sample into small pieces. A tweezer was used to 
transfer the small pieces of tissue on the edge of the blade inside individual M tubes with QIAzol 
Lysis Reagent, ensuring proper homogenization. Both the tweezer and scalpel were cleaned with 
0.5% NaOH between each sample.  
The tissue samples in the M tubes were then homogenized in the GentleMACS on program 
“RNA_02.01 (fresh tissue)”. The M tubes were then collected and placed on the lab bench for 5 
minutes at room temperature (15-25℃) after the finalized program. The content of each M tube was 
now a mix of cellular components including nuclei, membranes, mitochondria, RNA amongst other, 
which is referred to as homogenate. 
Thereafter, 200 µL of chloroform was added to each M tube, in order to separate the sample in 3 
phases; RNA, DNA and proteins. The M tubes got closed by a lid and vortexed for about 15 sec-
onds. The M tubes with homogenate were then left at room temperature for 3 minutes. The homoge-
nates were moved to a 1.5 mL tube from the kit and centrifuged at 12,000 g for 15 minutes at 4℃. 
After centrifugation, the content of the homogenate was separated in 3 phases: An upper aqueous 
phase with RNA, an interphase with DNA, and proteins are found either at the interphase or at the 
lower organic phase together with chloroform and other organic compounds as phenol and thiocya-
nate from the QIAzol Lysis Reagent. RNA comprises a 2’-hydroxyl group on its ribose sugar, 
which makes it more polar, which is why it gathers in the aqueous phase.  
 
After separating the homogenate into 3 phases, 300 µL of the upper aqueous phase of each tube was 
pipetted into new 2 mL tubes from the kit. Then, 450 µL (1:1.5) 100% ethanol were added to each 
of the 2 mL tubes and mixed by pipetting up and down. Subsequently, 600 µL of the individual 
samples were transferred to separate RNeasy Mini Spin Columns in a 1.5 mL collection tube. The 
lids on the tubes were closed, and the tubes were centrifuged at 8,000 g for 15 seconds at room tem-
perature, followed by the flow-through being discarded. The centrifugation was performed again 
with any remaining sample in the same respective RNeasy Mini Spin Columns, leaving the RNA 
bound to the column membrane. The DNase I and RDD buffer were collected from the freezer. 
Each of the columns were added 350 µL of RWT buffer and centrifuged at 8,000 g for 15 seconds 
at room temperature, and the flow-through was discarded. The RWT buffer comprises ethanol and 
guanidine salt, which degrades RNases whilst keeping RNA bound to the membrane [69].  
 
DNase I incubation mix was then prepared by gently mixing 10 µL DNase I stock solution with 70 
µL RDD Buffer with a pipette, per RNA extraction reaction. Subsequently, 77 µL of the DNase I 
==PAGE:44==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
43 
incubation mix were transferred to each of the RNeasy Mini Spin column membranes, directly on 
the membrane, and incubated at room temperature for 15 minutes. The DNase I incubation mix en-
sured that contaminating genomic DNA, was removed from the membranes. Then, 350 µL of RWT 
buffer was pipetted into each column and centrifuged at 8,000 g for 15 seconds at room tempera-
ture, and the flow-through was discarded. Another round of centrifugation was performed with 500 
µL RPE buffer, which was pipetted to each column and centrifuged at 8,000 g at room temperature 
for 15 seconds, and the flow-through was discarded. The RPE buffer ensured that remaining salts 
from prior applied buffers were removed from the membrane [70]. Thereafter, 500 µL RPE buffer 
was added once more to each column and centrifuged at 8,000 g for 2 minutes at room temperature, 
and the flow-through was discarded. The extended time in this centrifugation step was essential by 
drying the column membrane and thereby ensuring that no ethanol was carried over during the 
RNA elution, which otherwise could interfere with downstream reactions. The contents of the col-
umns were carefully transferred to separate new collection tubes, which were centrifuged at 8,000 g 
for 2 minutes, in terms of eliminating any remaining RPE Buffer.  
Elution of RNA was done by carefully transferring the content of the collection tubes to new 1.5 
mL collection tubes. Then, 50 µL of RNase-free H2O was added to each membrane and centrifuged 
at 8,000 g for 1 minute at room temperature.  
The quantity of the eluted RNA samples was determined by measuring the OD of the samples in a 
Nanodrop instrument at A260, since RNA absorbs light at 260 nm. In addition, A260/230 and 
A260/280 ratios were measured to find potential contaminating organic compounds, salts or pep-
tides interfering with the purity of the RNA samples. The extracted RNA samples were properly la-
beled with initials and number and stored at −80℃.  
 
6.2.1.2.2 RNA Extraction from Blood collected in PAXgene Blood RNA Tubes 
The extraction of RNA from blood in PAXgene Blood RNA Tubes was performed prior to this 
master thesis by other parts. The PAXgene Blood miRNA set (Qiagen #763134), and the RNase-
Free DNase set (Qiagen #79254) were utilized. The content of the kit specific for RNA extraction 
of PAXgene Blood is found in Table 12.1 in Appendix 2, and the protocol is found in Appendix 6. 
The PAXgene Blood RNA Tubes were handled differently than tissue samples. The PAX samples 
were centrifuged and washed with RNase-free H2O. The supernatant was removed, and the pellet 
was resuspended with BM1 resuspension buffer, which is used to digest cellular components. This 
is followed by addition of BM2 buffer and Proteinase K to degrade proteins in the sample. A 
PAXgene shredder spin column was used to homogenize the sample. The addition of isopropanol to 
the supernatant enhanced the binding condition of the RNA to the silica-membrane in the PAXgene 
Spin Column under centrifugation. Contaminating genomic DNA, was cleared by adding DNase I 
to the membrane. Afterwards, the wash buffers being BM3 and BM4 were used to clear the mem-
brane for residual cell components and reagents used in the process, e.g., isopropanol before eluting 
the RNA. Then, BR5 was used to elute the RNA sample from the membrane.  
 
The quantity of the eluted RNA samples was determined by measuring the OD of the samples in a 
Nanodrop instrument at A260, since RNA absorbs light at 260 nm. In addition, A260/230 and 
A260/280 ratios were measured to find potential contaminating organic compounds, salts or 
==PAGE:45==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
44 
peptides interfering with the purity of the RNA samples. The extracted RNA samples were properly 
labeled with initials and number and stored at −80℃.  
6.2.1.3 Bioanalyzer  
The quality of the RNA samples was measured by determining the RIN of the extracted RNA sam-
ples by using the Agilent 6000 Nano Kit (5067-1511) together with the Bioanalyzer system. The 
content of the aforementioned kit and the materials used for conducting the Bioanalyzer protocol 
are mentioned in Table 12.2, and the protocol can be found in Appendix 6. The RINs of several 
RNA samples were already determined by other parts prior to this thesis, however the RINs of 
LCA1, LCA2, LCA3, LCA4, HEVO731, HEVO 733, HEVO734, and HEVO739 were determined 
during the conduction of this thesis. 
The process initiated with equilibrating the RNA 6000 Dye Concentrate, gel matrix and marker at 
room temperature for 30 minutes. The dye concentrate was protected from light sources to prevent 
decomposition of the dye. Dilution samples with a total volume of 3 µL made of RNA samples di-
luted with RNase free H2O holding a concentration of 100 ng/µL were prepared. The amount of 
RNA was calculated as in Equation 6.1, and an overview of the RNA and RNase free H2O content 
in the dilution samples is found in Table 6.4.

The orange-colored row represents an RNA sample which could not be diluted to 100 ng/µL, since it already possessed 
an RNA concentration below 100 ng/µL, hence 3 µL of the respective samples was applied.  
The gel was filtered by pipetting 550 µL of the gel matrix into a spin filter and then centrifuged at 
1,500 g for 10 minutes, at room temperature. The filtered gel was then divided into 65 µL aliquots. 
The Gel-Dye Mix were prepared by vortexing the gel, and then 1 µL of the dye was transferred into 
the 65 µL aliquot of the filtered gel and mixed by pipetting up and down. The Gel-Dye Mix was 
centrifuged at 13,000 x g at room temperature for 10 minutes. Then, 3 µL of the diluted RNA 
==PAGE:46==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
45 
samples, and the vial of RNA Ladder were heated at 70℃ for 2 minutes and immediately cooled on 
ice. An RNA 6000 Nano Chip were placed on the priming station, and the plunger was positioned at 
1 mL. Afterwards, 9 µL of the Gel-Dye Mix were added to the well, which was marked with the let-
ter G. The priming station were closed, and the plunger was then pushed until the clip held it. The 
chip was then left for 30 seconds and released by pulling the handle. The plunger was left to rise by 
itself for approximately 5 seconds, thereafter the plunger was pulled back to the 1 mL position. 
Once again, the chip priming station was opened and 9 µL of Gel-Dye Mix were pipetted into the 
other two G wells. Subsequently, 5 µL of the marker were added to all sample and ladder wells. The 
ladder well was added 1 µL of the prepared ladder, whereas 1 µL of each sample were added to 
their own separate sample wells. The chip was positioned horizontally in the chip vortexer and vor-
texed for 1 minute at 2,400 rpm.  
In terms of avoiding decomposition of the RNA samples, the electrodes were decontaminated be-
fore initiating the program. 350 µL RNaseZAP was added to an electrode cleaner chip, which was 
then inserted in the Bioanalyzer and left for 1 minute. The chip was removed, and another electrode 
cleaner chip with 350 µL RNase-free H2O was inserted in the bioanalyzer and left for 10 seconds. 
The chip was then removed and the electrodes in the Bioanalyzer were left air-drying for another 10 
seconds. Then, the RNA Nanochip was put on the Bioanalyzer and the program “2100 expert Eu-
karyote Total RNA Nano” were initiated, and RIN values were determined.  
 
6.2.1.4 Synthesis of cDNA  
The cDNA synthesis of the RNA samples mentioned in Table 6.1Fejl! Henvisningskilde ikke fun-
det. was performed in two rounds with the QuantiTect Reverse Transcription Kit (Qiagen 
#205311). The content of the kit and the materials used for conducting the cDNA synthesis are rep-
resented in Table 12.2. 
Tissue samples, genomic DNA (gDNA) wipeout buffer (7x), RT, and RT Primer Mix were thawed 
on ice. In the first cDNA synthesis, 13 Eppendorf 0.5 mL tubes were labeled with a cDNA-number 
from ‘C1’ to ‘C12’, and the last tube was the -RT control which was labeled ‘C13(-RT)’. In the sec-
ond cDNA synthesis 7 tubes were labeled from ‘C14’ to ‘C19’, and ‘C20(-RT)’ as -RT controls. 
Table 6.5 holds information of the content of each C-tube. All the labeled tubes aimed to have a to-
tal of 250 ng extracted RNA sample, and a total volume of 14 µL. At first 1.5 µL gDNA wipeout 
buffer (7x) was added to each tube removing potential present contaminating gDNA. Subsequently, 
each tube was added different amounts of RNase-free H2O and RNA sample, depending on the indi-
vidual RNA sample’s concentrations of RNA. The applied volumes, in terms of reaching an amount 
of 250 ng of RNA in each tube, were calculated by the following Equation 6.2.

could then be collected and stored at -20℃.   
 
6.2.1.5 RT-qPCR Assay 
The RT-qPCR assay analyzed each primer pair in individual assays utilizing a RotorGene machine 
for amplification cycling and for conducting a melt curve. The applied primer pairs and references 
are mentioned in Table 6.3, and the applied materials used in the RT-qPCR assay are declared in 
Table 12.4.Table 12.2 
The cDNA samples from the 2 separate cDNA synthesis rounds were likewise separated in two 
groups under the RT-qPCR protocol. Group 1 comprised cDNA from lean and obese PAX, heart 
and SAT samples. Group 2 comprised cDNA from obese and lean liver and aorta samples.

as ‘F X’, with X being the number of the corresponding C-tube. Each F-tube was added 18 µL TE 
buffer, and 2 µL of the corresponding cDNA sample. For example 2 µL of C1 were transferred to 
F1, which already comprised 18 µL TE buffer, and vortexed. The same procedure was done with 
the rest of the cDNA samples. An overview of the 0.1x dilution series of tissue cDNA (F-tubes) is 
illustrated in Table 6.7.

Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
49 
A sample of the Forward primer (F (primer)) and the Reverse primer (R (primer)) at a concentration 
of 5 µM were made, in a new 1.5 mL Eppendorf tube labeled F+R. The tube was transferred 10 µL 
of each of the primer pairs, comprising F and R, and furthermore 180 µL of RNase-free H2O.  
The PCR mixes were made in the hood and were made by adding 112 µL of EvaGreen (20x), 168 
µL of F+R 5 µM, 364 µL of RNase-free H2O, and 700 µL of 2x PCR Master mix in a 1.5 mL Ep-
pendorf tube and mixing by pipetting.  
 
The RotorGene was turned on, strip tubes of 0.1 mL and matching caps were placed in a rack, and 
24 µL of the PCR Mix was transferred to each tube. Subsequently, 1 µL of S1-S5, S1-S5, and tripli-
cates of F-tube samples were added to separate tubes in the rack. An example of the rack setup of 
the tubes and corresponding content can be found in Appendix 7. 
The RT-qPCR and the melt curve were performed by placing the tubes in a circular rotor in the 
chamber of the RotorGene. The RotorGene was responsible for heating the tubes in the chamber by 
circulating air, creating a uniform temperature across all samples in terms of creating consistent am-
plification. Every tube passed the same optical detection point in the RotorGene, which ensured uni-
form collection of fluorescent signals when the tubes passed.  
The process in the RotorGene started with a hold phase, which heated the samples to 94℃ for 10 
minutes to activate the DNA polymerase. The hold phase was followed by a cycling phase, which 
included 40 cycles of heating the samples to 95℃ for 15 seconds and then cooling them to 60℃ for 
60 seconds. At 95℃ the cDNA denatured into single strands. At 60℃ the primers annealed to the 
complementary sequence in the cDNA, and DNA polymerase bound to the primer-cDNA template 
complex and extended the primer by synthesizing a new strand complementary to the cDNA tem-
plate. Each cycle amplified the amount of double stranded DNA referred to as the PCR product. 
The EvaGreen dye bound to the double stranded PCR product, and emitted green fluorescent sig-
nals when it got excited by the proper light source in the RotorGene. The optical detection point 
collected the green fluorescent signals, which created an amplification curve, representing the in-
creasing amount of amplified double stranded PCR product bound to EvaGreen per cycle. 
After the cycling phase, a melt phase was performed creating a melting curve. The melt phase 
heated the samples to 50℃ and continued increasing the temperature with 1℃  every 90 seconds, 
until the end temperature of 99℃ was reached.  
6.2.2 ELISA  
The ELISA assay targeted both ucOC and cOC by utilizing the Porcine Osteocalcin ELISA Kit 
(A74236), see Appendix 2 Table 12.5 for an overview of the ELISA kit content.  
Plasma samples and protein extracted heart, liver & SAT samples from both obese and lean Ossa-
baw miniature pigs were tested, see Table 6.2 for further information concerning the samples origin. 
The plasma samples and protein extracted heart, liver and SAT samples were made prior to this the-
sis. The plasma samples were prepared from blood samples, which had been collected in tubes con-
taining EDTA, which inhibited coagulation in the blood sample and created a stabilized whole 
blood sample. The blood samples were then centrifuged and the clear upper phase (supernatant), 
which did not contain any blood cells, however still blood clotting factors, were collected as the 
plasma samples. 
==PAGE:51==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
50 
The protein extractions of each sample were made by homogenizing ~130 mg of the respective tis-
sue sample in an M-tube with 900 µL RIPA solution (Thermo Scientific) and protease inhibitor 
cocktail Complete Ultra (Roche, 1 tablet per 10 mL buffer). Then the samples were lysed by utiliz-
ing the GentleMACS Dissociator each round took 80 seconds, and the number of rounds depended 
on when the respective samples were completely homogenized. The samples were incubated over-
night on ice. Then the samples were centrifuged for 15 minutes at 20,000 g at 4 ℃. The fat layer in 
each of the samples was removed. The supernatant of the samples with no visible fat layer were dia-
lyzed against PBS overnight at 4 ℃. A Nanodrop instrument was then utilized to measure the total 
protein concentration at 280 nm, in each of the protein extracted samples obtained from heart and 
liver tissue. The total protein concentration of SAT samples was found by using the Pierce™ BCA 
Protein Assay Kit (A55864 assay). The assay applied an alkaline medium where proteins reduced 
Cu!"-ions to Cu"-ions, which then formed a complex with bicinchoninic acid (BCA) which gener-
ated a purple color that absorbs light at 562 nm. The samples were measured at 562 nm by applying 
the Nanodrop instrument to obtain the total protein concentration of the SAT samples. 
 
The initial step in the ELISA was to prepare standard dilutions. Standard 0 was prepared by adding 
1 mL of Sample Dilution Buffer to one lyophilized Standard Vial and placed on the lab bench at 
room temperature for 10 minutes. Meanwhile 7 Eppendorf tubes were labeled with Standard 1- 
Standard 6 and a blank. Then standard 0 were mixed thoroughly and 300 µL were added in the 
Standard 1 tube together with 300 µL of Sample Dilution Buffer and mixed thoroughly. Then 300 
µL of Standard 1 were transferred to the Standard 2 tube together with 300 µL of Sample Dilution 
Buffer. The same procedure was applied to generate the rest of the standard samples in a 1:2 dilu-
tion series, which is shown in Table 6.9.

Figure 6.3 ELISA Microtiter Plate Layout.  
In the meanwhile, 750 mL of wash buffer was prepared in a graduated cylinder by adding 30 mL of 
wash buffer (25x) concentrate to 720 mL deionized water and then covered with parafilm until use. 
Furthermore, the Biotinylated Detection Antibody solution was prepared. 100 µL of Biotinylated 
Detection Antibody was diluted with 10 mL of Antibody Dilution Buffer to create a 1:100 dilution.  
After the 90 minutes incubation time, the plate sealer was removed and the liquid from the plate 
was decanted into the sink, and the plate was washed 2 times with the prepared wash buffer. Each 
wash step consisted of pipetting 350 µL of wash buffer into each well with a multi-channel pipette, 
then left to soak for 1-2 minutes, and thereafter the wash buffer was completely removed by decant-
ing. To ensure that the wells did not contain any remaining wash buffer after decanting, then the 
plate was blotted towards paper towels, however the wells were never fully dried at any point. After 
the washing steps, then 100 µL of the 1:100 Biotinylated Detection Antibody solution was added to 
each well. The plate was then sealed with a plate sealer and incubated for 60 minutes at 37℃.  
During the incubation time, the HRP-Streptavidin Conjugate (SABC) solution was prepared. 100 
µL of HRP-Streptavidin Conjugate (SABC) were diluted with 10 mL of SABC Dilution Buffer to 
create a 1:100 dilution. The plate sealer was removed, and the plate was washed 3 times with wash 
buffer after finalized incubation. Then 100 µL of the 1:100 HRP-Streptavidin Conjugate (SABC) 
solution was added to each well. The plate was then sealed with a plate sealer and incubated for 30 
minutes at 37℃. This was followed by 5 washing steps with washing buffer, and then 90 µL of 
TMB substrate was added to each well. The plate was then sealed and left for incubation at 37℃ for 
20 minutes. After 20 minutes, several wells had turned blue. All wells had 50 µL of Stop Solution 
added, and the blue colored wells turned yellow in response to the Stop Solution. The OD of each 
well was then measured by a microplate reader set to 450 nm.

7 Results & Discussion  
7.1 RNA Extraction & cDNA Synthesis 
RNA Samples were extracted from various selected tissue obtained from both obese and lean Ossa-
baw miniature pigs, mentioned in Table 6.1. Each of the extracted RNA samples were measured at 
A260 and further analyzed by the Nanodrop instrument to provide the RNA concentration in each 
sample. The purity of the RNA samples was determined by measuring and calculating the ratio be-
tween A260/A230 and A260/A280. The results are listed in Table 7.1.  
 
The RNA concentrations of the various samples differ quite much, and it is possible that the type of 
tissue, the quality of tissue samples, and the RNA extraction procedure all might affect the final 
RNA concentration of the samples. As an example, the SAT samples all possess a quite low RNA 
concentration, in comparison to the other RNA samples. An A260/280 ratio of ~2.0 is preferred to 
validate the purity of RNA from protein contamination [71]. Most of the RNA samples possess an 
A260/280 above 2.0. Although the A260/280 ratio of CATV23 and HEVO 697 are slightly below 
2.0, however in close proximity to the preferred value, suggesting that there should not be any ma-
jor contamination in these RNA samples. The ratio of A260/230 is preferred to possess a value be-
tween 1.8-2.2, however the majority of the RNA samples do not meet this [71]. The only samples 
possessing an A260/230 ratio in the preferred range are HEVO709, -722, -733, and -739. The ma-
jority of the remaining RNA samples’ A260/230 ratios are below 1.8, however, HEVO732, and -
739’s A260/230 ratio are slightly above 2.2. The reason behind the slightly higher A260/230 ratio 
could be due to several reasons, however it is unplausible that it is caused by contamination of or-
ganic compounds, but rather instrumental variation in the Nanodrop instrument. The rest of the 
samples with an A260/230 ratio below 1.8 are most likely contaminated with organic compounds. 
The contamination of the RNA samples could have occurred in the extraction of RNA. The RNA 
extraction was performed with the use of the phenol/guanidine based QIAzol Lysis Reagent to ho-
mogenize the cells in the tissue samples. Phenol and guanidine are both organic compounds absorb-
ing light at 230 nm, which might still be present in the majority of the RNA samples with an 
A260/230 ratio below 1.8 [72]. Furthermore, a low RNA concentration contributes to a low 
A260/230 ratio, which corresponds well with the majority of the SAT samples shown in Table 7.1, 
the supplier of the kits applied in this thesis, Qiagen, has published data disclosing that a A260/230 
ratio does not necessarily affect the results of the downstream RT-qPCR assay[73].  
 
The quality of the RNA samples was assessed by the Bioanalyzer instrument providing a RIN for 
each RNA sample, listed in Table 7.1 The RIN can range between 1-10, where a low RIN signifies 
a poor RNA quality and a high RIN reflects high RNA quality. A RIN > 6 is considered preferable 
to validate the integrity and thereby quality of the given RNA sample. The majority of the obtained 
RIN results of the RNA samples investigated in this thesis are above the quality threshold of 6. 
However, the RNA sample of LCA 2 possess a poor RIN of solely 3.3, meaning that this RNA sam-
ple might be highly degraded. The reduced RNA quality of LCA 2 could be due to incomplete ho-
mogenization in the RNA extraction, amongst other reasons. However, it is worth mentioning that 
the fresh aorta tissue was challenging to handle and further cut into small pieces, due to its special 
==PAGE:54==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
53 
composition of being soft, hard, and slippery at the same time. However, when aorta tissue is frozen 
it is stiff, in comparison to the other tissue samples. Challenges in the homogenization during the 
RNA extraction could have been affected by the special tissue type of aorta and could have affected 
the RNA in LCA 2. Furthermore, the low RNA concentration of LCA 2 points towards that the ho-
mogenization in the RNA extraction of the aorta tissue in LCA 2 might have been performed unsuc-
cessfully.  
After validating the purity, quantity, and quality of the RNA samples, then cDNA was synthesized 
to serve as template for the RT-qPCR assays.

7.2 RT-qPCR Assay 
The RT-qPCR assays were conducted to determine the RQ of the target genes across certain cDNA 
samples, presented in Table 6.5, by detecting fluorescent signals increasing in correlation with the 
amount of double stranded PCR product per cycle. Primer pairs for MGP, OC and UCMA were de-
signed both before and during the execution of the RT-qPCR assays. This was essential, since there 
was no guarantee that all primer pairs were specific enough to the target MGP, OC or UCMA DNA. 
However, it was necessary to run a full RT-qPCR assay for each primer pair to be able to evaluate 
the PCR efficiency and primer specificity.  
The results from each RT-qPCR assay came out as two separate curves, one being the amplification 
curve and the other being the melt curve. The amplification curve was used to identify the Cq val-
ues, at which the fluorescent signal reaches the threshold, of each cDNA sample. The melt curve is 
used to determine the primer specificity of the product formed by determining the product Tm value 
of each sample, reflecting the temperature at which 50% of the PCR product had melted. In order to 
remain unbiased of the quantification results, then the melt curve analysis was performed prior to 
examining the Cq values in the amplification curves.  
The analysis initiated with the melt curve obtained from the RT-qPCR assay conducted with the 
MGP 229 primer pair on cDNA samples from group 1. The corresponding melt curve is shown in 
Figure 7.1. 
 
 
 
Figure 7.1. Melt Curve obtained from the RT-qPCR assay conducted with the MGP 229 primer pair on cDNA samples 
from group 1. 
MGP 229 Melt Curve (Group 1 cDNA) 
==PAGE:56==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
55 
The melt curve in Figure 7.1 represents the rate of change in fluorescence per degree of tempera-
ture, depicted as dF/dT along the y-axis, whilst the increasing temperature is found along the x-axis.  
Detection of some signals are found between 50 – 80 ℃, and a more distinct peak is found at ≈84 
℃, assumingly being the PCR product Tm. Samples within the red, light pink-, pink-, yellow- and 
orange-colored curves appear as broader peaks, double peaks, and some samples do not even form 
any peak. It is not desirable to identify double peaks or broader low intensity peaks in the melt 
curve results, since it may indicate primer dimer formation, unspecific primer pairs resulting in un-
specific PCR product formation, poor quality of the cDNA samples, or even initially poor quality of 
the RNA samples. Lacking results are assumingly a result of low quantity of PCR products, due to 
poor amplification during the RT-qPCR assay, which might be revealed in the samples’ correspond-
ing Cq-values. However, it is essential to state that various factors can affect the PCR product Tm, 
and before performing further analysis of the melt curve in Figure 7.1, then some of the key factors 
affecting the PCR product Tm is stated below. 
 
It has been mentioned earlier that longer PCR products possess a higher product Tm, than shorter 
PCR products. Furthermore, a higher product Tm is found in PCR products comprising a higher ra-
tio of Guanine and Cytosine base-pairs, forming 3 H-bonds, than Adenine and Thymine base-pairs, 
forming 2 H-bonds. A longer PCR product usually comprises an increased number of H-bonds than 
shorter PCR products. An increased number of H-bonds creates more stable PCR products, which 
then require more energy to denature, which leads to an increased product Tm.  
In addition, the melt curve results can be affected by factors including the annealing temperature, 
the splice-site variants, and the primer pair- and salt (e.g., Mg2+) concentration in the PCR mix.  
The proper PCR annealing temperature differs from each primer pair, which is also affected by the 
GC% ratio and the length of the primers. In this thesis, the primer pairs were designed to have a pri-
mer Tm between 59-61 ℃, since the annealing temperature was set to 60 ℃ in the PCR setup. The 
primer Tm is the temperature needed for 50% of the primers and their template strand to form a du-
plex [74]. The annealing phase is the step after the denaturation phase at 95℃, where the double 
stranded PCR products should have been denatured to single strands. The primer Tm is then reached 
to create the exact temperature for 50% of the remaining primers to anneal. An annealing tempera-
ture, which is too low will cause unspecific binding, which potentially would create various PCR 
product Tm values, due to numerous unspecific PCR products. In case of an annealing temperature 
being too high, this could give rise to lacking formation of PCR products, and minimal product Tm 
data. The information concerning the individual primer Tm for each of the designed primers are 
stated in the top of each Primer3 output illustrated in Appendix 4. 
The splice-site variants of each genetic sequence can affect the product Tm, since binding sites may 
differ due to different splice-sites, and thereby differing sequence composition. The design of pri-
mer pairs in this thesis took the identified splice variants according to Ensembl into account. How-
ever, there might exist several more unidentified splice variants, which are expressed differently 
from tissue to tissue. Unidentified splice variants, which the design of primer pairs did not consider, 
could give rise to the formation of slightly different PCR products, which could affect the product 
Tm depending on the cDNA sample.  
==PAGE:57==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
56 
The concentration of primer pairs in the PCR Mix may also impact the melt curve results, since too 
high primer concentrations could give rise to unspecific binding resulting in formation of unspecific 
PCR products, and potentially give rise to primers binding to each other forming primer-dimers. 
Formation of unspecific PCR products and primer dimers will give rise to various product Tm melt 
peaks, including double peaks.  
The active site of DNA polymerase requires Mg!"-ions as co-factor to catalyze the phosphodiester 
bond formed between the primers 3’ OH groups and the phosphate groups of the dNTPs in the elon-
gation process. Insufficient concentrations of Mg!"-ions will therefore lead to inefficient DNA pol-
ymerase activity and small amounts of PCR product. In addition, Mg!"-ions can stabilize the du-
plex formed by binding to the phosphate group on the backbones and thereby stabilize the negative 
charges. A high concentration of Mg!"-ions in the PCR Mix could therefore give rise to unspecific 
duplexes of primers and cDNA templates, due to the increased stability between primer and cDNA. 
Overall, various factors can interfere with the results of the product Tm peaks in the melt curve. It is 
possible to calculate the product Tm for each PCR product; however an exact calculation requires 
knowledge of the exact composition of the 2x Master Mix applied in the PCR Mix, which was not 
accessible from the supplier in this thesis.  
 
Returning to Figure 7.1, the majority of the product melting peaks are found at ≈84 ℃, which pre-
sumably may be the PCR product Tm. It is worth mentioning that nearly all data appearing as outli-
ers are cDNA obtained from PAX samples including 1 cDNA obtained from SAT. The RNA purity 
and RIN of the respective PAX samples, shown in Table 7.1, do not reflect any poor purity or qual-
ity of RNA. However, the majority of SAT samples possess low RNA concentrations and 
A260/A230 ratios. Various reasons could give rise to the outliers, and starting with the sample ob-
tained from SAT, then it is solely one SAT cDNA sample which appears as outlier, wherefore it is 
most likely a pipetting mistake that could cause lack of SAT cDNA sample in the RT-qPCR assay. 
The rest of the outliers are found in the samples made from PAX, and assumingly this could suggest 
the existence of unidentified splice-site variants expressed in PAX. Despite that the primer pair 
MGP 229 was made to fit for both splice variants being Q8MJ39 and A0A4X1V4A6, then there 
could be additional MGP splice variants with potential increased expression in PAX, which have 
not been identified yet. This could potentially result in the primer pairs being more unspecific in 
these cDNA samples expressing potential unidentified splice variants of MGP.  
In this thesis, all the melt data showing significant double-, broad peaks or peaks appearing as outli-
ers from the range of +/- 1℃ from the majority of the PCR products peaks, the product Tm, were re-
moved from further analysis, since these samples might had comprised unspecific PCR products.

Figure 7.3. Amplification Curve of MGP 229 with cDNA from Group 1. 
 
The graphs created in the amplification curve is based on the normalized fluorescence, along the y-
axis, per PCR cycle, shown on the x-axis. The Cq-value of each sample is the result of the sample’s 
normalized fluorescence intensity passing the predetermined threshold value at 0.05, at a certain 
number of cycles. An appropriate threshold value should be defined to lay within the exponential 
phase, which should be parallel between all samples in the same RT-qPCR assay. Furthermore, the 
MGP 229 Amplification Curve (Group 1 cDNA) 
MGP 229 Data Curated Amplification  Curve (Group 1 cDNA) 
==PAGE:60==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
59 
threshold value should be adjusted to lay above the background noise. The background noise is 
found in the first cycles of an RT-qPCR amplification curve and is potentially created due to exter-
nal circumstances such as e.g., light leaking. As a part of the RT-qPCR set-up, the data from the 
first cycles between 1-10 were eliminated. In Figure 7.3, the first Cq values is found at cycle 20 by 
the S1 sample, illustrated as dark green in the curve. A low Cq-value is the result of an increased 
amount of the target DNA in the sample, and a high Cq-value is the result of a low amount of the 
target DNA. High Cq-values are always favored as results from the control samples comprising the 
None-Template Control (NTC) and the -RT sample. The NTC sample comprised PCR mix without 
addition of any cDNA samples. The NTC sample was essential to ensure that the detected signals 
were created upon PCR products as the result of binding specificity between the primer pairs and 
their target cDNA, and not contaminants in the PCR mix [75]. The -RT sample lacked the reverse 
transcriptase enzyme in the cDNA synthesis. This means that the -RT sample comprised of RNA, 
which DNA polymerase could not bind to. High Cq-values from the -RT sample could be a sign of 
gDNA leftovers from the performance of RNA extraction, where DNase was used to remove 
gDNA. It could also be contamination during the cDNA synthesis, or potentially when pipetting di-
luted cDNA samples in the separate tubes containing PCR mix before insertion in the Rotorgene 
machine. The exact quantitative Cq-values from the RT-qPCR assay conducted with MGP 229 pri-
mer pair on cDNA samples from group 1 are found below in Table 7.2. 
 
 
Table 7.2. Cq-Values from RT-qPCR with MGP 229 and cDNA from group 1. The orange fields represent the excluded 
data of the outliers in found in Figure 7.1.

comparison to the dynamic range of the dilution series (S1-S5 & S1-S5), and in general to the ma-
jority of the other samples. This indicates that the RT-qPCR assay had not been contaminated either 
in the production of PCR mix or by gDNA in the performance of the cDNA samples.

All RT-qPCR assays with individual primer pairs were evaluated with a similar approach as de-
scribed above with MGP 229 + cDNA samples from group 1. All amplification and melt curves are 
found in Appendix 8. Additionally, tables with quantitative data of the Tm- and Cq-values from the 
RT-qPCR assays showing product specificity are included in Appendix 8.  
The evaluation of the primer pairs’ specificity led to exclusion of data obtained in the RT-qPCR as-
says conducted with certain primer pairs. The excluded primer pairs tested with one group of cDNA 
were in general not tested with the other group of cDNA samples.  
 
The primer pairs MGP 229 and MGP 230 were designed to target the MGP cDNA. The evaluation 
of the MGP 229 primer pair with cDNA from group 1 has been thoroughly explained above, and the 
selected data was validated to continue to further calculation of the RQ of MGP. The melt curve ob-
tained from the RT-qPCR MGP 229 + cDNA from group 2 forms a peak at 84 ℃, however the melt 
data obtained from S4+S4 and S5+S5 of the standard dilutions and F14 (Lean Liver cDNA) were 
removed since they formed small double peaks after the assumed product Tm at 84℃. 
The remaining data from MGP 229 + cDNA from group 2 was applied in the further determination 
of RQ of MGP.  
 
The melt data obtained from the MGP 230 assay with cDNA from group 1 illustrated a peak at 
82℃, which is assumingly the product Tm. However, this assay possessed decreased specificity, due 
to the presence of an increased amount of double- and broad peaks found in the corresponding melt 
curve. The outliers were the samples from lean and obese PAX cDNA (F3, F5, F6, F7 & F10), and 
from obese heart cDNA (F9), which are excluded from further analysis. Once again, the unspecific 
products are primarily found in the cDNA from PAX tissue, which supports the reasoning to as-
sume that unidentified splice site variants of MGP may be expressed in PAX tissue, as discussed 
with the melt results obtained from MGP 229 + cDNA from group 1.  
In contrast, the melt data obtained from the MGP 230 assay with cDNA from group 2 had increased 
specificity in terms of the data forming one distinct peak at 82.5 ℃. S5 was the only sample re-
moved, since the product peak from S5 was found at 81℃, and therefore not in the range of +/-1℃ 
from the product Tm.  
 
The melt curve obtained from the OC 231 RT-qPCR assay performed with cDNA from group 1 did 
not show any distinct melt peak, hence OC 231 being unspecific and the data reflects various PCR 
products or even primer dimers. The same is seen in the melt curve obtained from the OC 232 RT-
qPCR assay with cDNA from group 1, which does not show any single melt peak. It was decided to 
exclude the data obtained from the RT-qPCR assays conducted with the primer pairs OC 231 and 
OC 232 with cDNA from group 1. Furthermore, neither OC 231 nor OC 232 were tested with 
cDNA from group 2.  
 
The discontinuation of OC 231 and OC 232 led to the new primer designs of OC 235, OC 236 and 
OC 237. OC 235, OC 236, and OC 237 were initially tested on cDNA from group 2. The melt 
curves obtained from OC 235 and OC 236 were as unspecific as OC 231 and OC 232, since they 
lacked a distinct melt peak.

The OC 237 RT-qPCR assay conducted with cDNA from group 2 created melt data comprising

The primer pairs targeting the cDNA of UCMA were UCMA 233 and UCMA 234. The UCMA 233 
RT-qPCR assay conducted with cDNA samples fromgroup 1 formed three distinct peaks and a 
number of additional small double peaks which indicated low specificity of UCMA 233, and the re-
sults from this assay were not used to determine the RQ of UCMA. Moreover, UCMA 233 was not 
tested with cDNA from group 2.  
The UCMA 234 RT-qPCR assay conducted with cDNA from group 1 formed a melt curve with 
broad peaks in the range between 75-78℃. Furthermore, detection of signals from several small ad-
ditional double-peaks at lower temperatures were present, which indicated low specificity.  
Despite the low specificity of the UCMA 234 assay conducted with cDNA from group 1, then a 
second trial of an additional UCMA 234 RT-qPCR assay was performed with cDNA from group 1. 
This was performed since the first UCMA 234 assay formed melt peaks at a narrower temperature 
range, than the melt data in the UCMA 233 melt curve. However, the second melt curve did not in-
dicate increased specificity, and an RT-qPCR assay with UCMA 234 and cDNA from group 2 was 
not conducted. Neither of the RT-qPCR data obtained from the assays performed with UCMA 233 
nor UCMA 234 could be used to determine the RQ of UCMA in the tested samples. 
 
The reference primer pairs were YWHAZ 156 and PPIA 127. YWHAZ 156 RT-qPCR assays were 
conducted with both cDNA from group 1 and group 2, and both separate melt curves had a Tm 
around 83 ℃. However, S5+S5 from both dilution series in both assays formed broad peaks and 
were removed from the standard curves. YWHAZ 156 was applied as reference for the RT-qPCR 
assays conducted with both cDNA from group 1 and 2. 
 
The melt curve obtained from the RT-qPCR assay performed with PPIA 127 + cDNA from group 1 
created a single melt peak 85℃, which assumingly was the product Tm. Certain samples did not cre-
ate any melt data, these were the cDNA samples from the dilution series’ S4, lean PAX (F1 & F6), 
furthermore, the melt data from obese SAT (F8) created a broad curve with several small peaks. 
The corresponding Cq values indicated that there might have been mistakes in pipetting or similar, 
since F1 did not produce sufficient fluorescence to obtain a Cq value, whilst S4, F6 and F8 had sim-
ilar Cq-values to the NTC and the -RT samples. This Indicated that no PCR products was formed in 
the respective samples. Even though that PPIA 127 was not as specific as YWHAZ 156, then PPIA

7.2.1 RT-qPCR Data Analysis - Relative Quantification  
The average of the Cq-values obtained from each dilution sample (S1-S5 & S1-S5), comprising dif-
ferent concentrations of pooled cDNA from group 1 or 2, are utilized as data for creating standard 
curves of each RT-qPCR assay. The standard curve is created upon the relation between the Cq-val-
ues per log transformed concentration of pooled cDNA in each sample from the dilution series.  
The results obtained from the cDNA samples from groups 1 & 2 cannot be compared, since the di-
lution series in each group is created from a pooled sample representing each cDNA sample in the 
respective groups. Data can therefore solely be compared within the same group, and not across the 
two groups. 
The standard curves in Figure 7.4 & Figure 7.5, are created by the specific and valid data, see Table 
7.3. The standard curve for the valid RT-qPCR assays conducted with cDNA from group 1 is found 
in Figure 7.4, whilst Figure 7.5 comprises the standard curve for the valid RT-qPCR assays con-
ducted with cDNA from group 2. The standard curve data for the curves below is found in Appen-
dix 9.

samples from group 1. The slope of the trendline obtained from the standard curve is used to deter-

Equation 7.1 
The PCR Efficiency of the RT-qPCR assays performed with the references, YWHAZ 156 and PPIA 
127 possess PCR efficiencies of 101% and 103%, respectively. However, MGP 229 possess a PCR 
efficiency of 115% and MGP 230 a PCR efficiency of 116%. This means that both references fall 
within the ideal range of PCR efficiency. However, the MGP 229 and MGP 230 assays both pos-
sess PCR efficiencies above 110%. In general, there is no such thing as efficiency being above 
100%. In an ideal RT-qPCR assay, every cDNA template can create one extra PCR product per cy-
cle, which leads to a perfect doubling each cycle. However, if the PCR efficiency is determined to 
be above 100% then this could indicate technical artifacts such as primer-dimers, an error in the 5-
fold dilution series giving rise to a too steep slope, or non-specific primers. The prior selection of 
specific data from the melt curves would most likely have removed potential noise in terms of pri-
mer-dimers and non-specific data. The tendency of the slightly increased PCR efficiency is mutual 
in both MGP 229 and MGP 230. In both amplification curves it is seen that the curves of S5+S5 
and S4+S4 visually lies in close proximity in comparison to the spread between the other samples in 
the dilution series. In theory a perfect slope would be found if the Cq-values increased by 
log!(5) = 2.32 at every 5-fold diluted sample. However, this is not entirely the case for either 
MGP 229 or MGP 230. Especially, the difference in Cq value between S5+S5 and S4+S4 is slightly 
lower than the value of 2.32 for both, see Appendix 8. This means that S5+S5 in both MGP 229 and 
MGP 230 did not follow the anticipated exponential doubling behavior. Therefore, S5+S5 are re-
moved from the MGP 229 and MGP 230 standard curves, and the new standard curves with addi-
tional trendlines are shown below in Figure 7.6.

now have an ideal PCR efficiency of 101% and 109%, respectively. The remaining Cq-values from 
the samples in each of the valid RT-qPCR assays tested with cDNA from group 1 are efficiency 
corrected to create comparable RT-qPCR data across cDNA from group 1. However, before effi-
ciency correction of the Cq values from group 1’s cDNA samples, then the PCR efficiency of the 
samples in group 2 will be determined.  
 
The standard curves found in Figure 7.5 are related to the valid RT-qPCR assays performed with 
the primer pairs MGP 229, MGP 230, and the reference YWHAZ 156 with cDNA samples from 
group 1. The trendlines in all the standard curves in Figure 7.5 do all possess an R2=1.00, which in-
dicates a consistent behavior between the Cq-values and the known concentrations in the dilution 
samples. The PCR efficiencies of the assays conducted with MGP 229, MGP 230, and YWHAZ 
156, tested with cDNA from group 2 are all calculated with the use of Equation 5.7, and the values 
are shown below in Equation 7.3.

The PCR Efficiency of the RT-qPCR assays performed with MGP 230 and YWHAZ 156 possess 
ideal PCR efficiencies of 103% and 100%, respectively. However, MGP 229 possess a PCR effi-
ciency of 115%. As aforementioned, the selection of specific melt data in the melt curves would 
most likely have removed potential noise in terms of primer-dimers and non-specific data. The dif-
ference value between the average S1, S2 and S3 are 2.05, and that does not comply with the ideal 
difference between the samples in the dilution series of 2.32 per 5-fold sample, as mentioned pri-
orly. The standard curve belonging to MGP 229 + cDNA from group 2 does solely have 3 data 
points, because S4+S4 and S5+S5 were removed due to poor melt specificity. Despite the slightly 
increased PCR efficiency of MGP 229 + cDNA from group 2, then it is decided to proceed the fur-
ther efficiency correction with the Cq values obtained from MGP 229 + cDNA from group 2. The 
standard curve must comprise minimum 3 data points, and therefore it is not possible to investigate 
whether one or more data points could be removed, due to lack of exponential behavior. Although, 
the results obtained from MGP 229 + cDNA samples from group 2 should be analyzed with respect 
to the unideal PCR efficiency.  
 
The remaining Cq-values from the samples in each of the valid RT-qPCR assay tested with cDNA 
from group 1 and 2 were then efficiency corrected to create comparable data across the cDNA 
tested. Since the Cq value of each sample were determined as triplets, then the average Cq-value of 
the samples showing specificity in each triplet were calculated and utilized for efficiency correction 
by using the Equation 5.8. The efficiency corrected Cq-values (𝐂𝐪𝐞𝐟𝐟) is shown below together with 
the calculated PCR efficiencies in Table 7.4 & Table 7.5.

The negative ΔCq-values are results of an increased fraction of the target gene in the respective 
samples in comparison to the references. The ΔCq-values were then transformed to linear scale in 
order to determine the RQ by finding the separate fold changes between the highest ΔCq-value and 
the remaining ΔCq-values. This will ensure each sample’s RQ of target DNA is set relative to the 
baseline sample comprising the highest ΔCq-value. The samples comprising the highest ΔCq value 
from MGP 229 + cDNA from group 1 was F3, and F10 from MGP 230, which can be seen in Table 
7.6. The samples comprising the highest ΔCq value from both MGP 229 and MGP 230 + cDNA 
from group 2 was F18, which can be seen in Table 7.7.

Figure 7.8. Overview of the fold Change of Average MGP RQ in (Obese/Lean) PAX & SAT samples from MGP 229 & 
MGP 230 RT-qPCR assays. 
The calculated fold changes between the obese and lean PAX or SAT samples are consistent with 
the previous mentioned tendencies.  
In the PAX samples conducted with MGP 229, it was seen that the obese tissue comprised 51.5% 
lower RQ of MGP, than the lean PAX samples, in average. The same tendency was seen in the 
PAX samples tested with MGP 230, where the RQ of MGP across the obese PAX samples were 
53% lower than the RQ of MGP as average on the lean PAX comprised. In general, it seems like 
the RQ of MGP is affected, and thereby potentially the expression is regulated by obesity in terms 
of decreasing the expression of MGP in relation with obesity, since the negative fold change be-
tween obese and lean samples are consistent across both MGP 229 and MGP 230 RT-qPCR assays. 
 
In the SAT samples conducted with MGP 229 it was seen that the obese tissue comprised 42% 
more RQ of MGP than the lean SAT. Moreover, the obese SAT samples tested with MGP 230 had 
a 26.6% increased RQ of MGP than the lean tissue. Despite the positive fold change between obese 
SAT and lean SAT differ in numbers across the MGP 229 and MGP 230 assays, then the RQ of 
MGP appears to consistently increase in obese SAT in comparison to lean SAT, in both assays. 
This indicates that there might be an enhanced regulation of MGP expression in SAT tissue as re-
sponse to obesity. It is mentioned in 5.1.1 Subcutaneous Fat Tissue that MGP is thought to possess 
a role in adipogenesis, which possibly could be the reason to the increased RQ of MGP. However, 
this is not investigated in this thesis, but it is notable that the RQ of MGP is higher in obese SAT 
than in lean SAT.

The RQ of MGP in heart tissue could solely be found in obese tissue from pig 13 and pig 25, since 
there were no lean heart tissue references. However, since all cDNA samples from group 1 had been 
efficiency adjusted by utilization of the same dilution series made from pooled cDNA from group 1, 
then it was possible to compare the RQ of MGP in heart tissue against the other tissues. In other 
words, it was not possible to gather information whether the MGP expression in heart tissue could 
be regulated in connection with obesity but the RQ of MGP could be compared cross-tissue to in-
vestigate the differences across tissue types. The cross-tissue fold change of the average RQ be-
tween obese heart sample and the remaining lean or obese PAX and SAT are found in Figure 7.9. 
 
 
 
 
Figure 7.9. Cross-Tissue Fold Change of the Average MGP RQ in Obese Heart Tissue versus (Obese/Lean) PAX & SAT 
samples from MGP 229 & MGP 230 RT-qPCR assays. 
The calculated cross-tissue fold changes indicate that the obese heart samples exhibited a larger RQ 
of MGP compared to both lean and obese PAX or SAT samples. 
The obese heart samples possessed a 542.3% and 1146.3 % increased RQ of MGP than the lean 
PAX samples tested in RT-qPCR assays with MGP 229 and MGP 230, respectively. The RQ of 
MGP in the obese heart samples were 1224% and 2549.3% higher, in comparison to obese PAX 
samples tested in RT-qPCR assays with MGP 229 and 230, respectively.  
The fold change between the average RQ of the obese heart samples and the lean SAT stated an in-
creased RQ of MGP in obese heart tissue. The RT-qPCR conducted with MGP 229 resulted in 
obese heart samples having 63.4% higher RQ of MGP than lean SAT, and 15.1% higher RQ of 
MGP than obese SAT. The RT-qPCR conducted with MGP 230 resulted in obese heart samples 
having 50.4% higher RQ of MGP than lean SAT, and 18.8% higher RQ of MGP than obese SAT.

Figure 7.11. Overview of the fold Change of Average MGP RQ in (Obese/Lean) liver & aorta tissue samples from MGP 
229 & MGP 230 RT-qPCR assays. 
The calculated average RQ of MGP fold changes between the obese and lean liver or aorta samples 
are consistent with the previous mentioned tendencies.  
In the liver samples conducted with MGP 229, it was seen that the obese tissue comprised 58.7% 
higher RQ of MGP, than the lean liver samples. However, the opposite tendency was found in the 
liver samples tested with MGP 230, where the RQ of MGP across the obese liver samples were 
1.6% lower than the RQ of MGP in the lean liver. Despite the contradictive results between the 
MGP 229 and MGP 230 assays, then there is no clear tendency on how the RQ of MGP in the liver 
is affected by obesity. These results must therefore lead to an inconclusive result since no assump-
tions can be drawn.  
 
The RQ of MGP in the obese aorta samples tested in RT-qPCR conducted with MGP 229 and MGP 
230 had a 42.8% and 42.6% respectively higher RQ of MGP, than the lean aorta samples. The iden-
tical results of increased RQ of MGP in both assays suggests that obesity affects the aorta tissue to 
express more MGP. Aorta is the largest blood vessel in the body, and therefore atherosclerotic 
plaque formation could have been present in the specific obese samples used in this thesis. In rela-
tion with the RQ of MGP found to be ~40% higher in obese aorta samples contra lean aorta sam-
ples, then an induced expression of MGP could potentially be a reaction to inhibit calcification in a 
local atherosclerotic plaque development in the aorta tissue. However, the aforementioned assump-
tion cannot be concluded definitively by these results.

7.3 ELISA  
The data obtained from the ELISA were the measured absorbance values at 450 nm red by a micro-
plate reader. The raw data obtained in the ELISA is found in Appendix 10. The raw data obtained 
from the standard dilutions, and the standard curve of the standard dilutions are found below in  
Figure 7.12. 
 
 
 
 
Figure 7.12. The OC ELISA Standard Curve.  
The x-axis represents the OC concentrations (ng/mL), and the y-axis represents the values measured at A450. 
The limit of detection (LOD) was determined by the microplate reader by being the average of the 
blanks’ A450 values, which was 0.7 as illustrated in Figure 7.12 . The samples possessing an A450 
value below 0.7 were excluded and denoted as “<min”. Furthermore, the lower quantification limit 
(LQL) is found at 0.63 ng/mL, since the lowest concentration in the dilution series is 0.315 ng/mL 
which has to be multiplied by two to get the true LQL of the samples which were diluted with a fac-
tor 1:2. This means that samples possessing a concentration below 0.63 ng/mL were not able to be 
detected properly, due to background noise, hence being invalid.  
The trendline in the standard curve possesses an ideal R2=0.99. The equation of the linear trendline 
is found in Equation 7.4.

Equation 7.4. The linear trendline equation of ELISA standard curve. 
The equation of the trendline can be used to determine the OC concentration in a certain sample by 
isolating “Conc. OC” as in Equation 7.5. 
Conc. OC = A450 value + 0.05
0.12
 
Equation 7.5 
The concentration of OC was then calculated by inserting each sample’s respective A450 value in  
Equation 7.5 and multiplying by 2, since all samples were diluted 1:2 with Sample Dilution Buffer. 
In order to compare the amount of OC across the samples, then the concentrations had to be normal-
ized. The amount of protein might differ from individual to individual and from tissue to tissue, due 
to biological variation. Normalization was done by dividing each of the determined concentrations 
of OC (ng/mL) with the total protein concentration of the same sample (µg/mL). This resulted in a 
ratio of OC (ng) per total protein (µg) of each sample, which could be used to compare the ratio of 
OC across the tissue samples.  
 
The A450 values, the calculated concentration of OC in ng/mL, and the ratio of OC per total protein 
in the samples are found in Table 7.10.

In Table 7.10 it is found that all plasma samples possessed an A450 below the LOD (<0.7), and the 
concentration of OC in plasma could not be determined at all in this assay. This could possibly indi-
cate that the plasma samples had a low biological concentration of OC, which were not detectable 
by this ELISA kit. Although, other factors could also give rise to the low A450 in the plasma sam-
ples, such as the fact that the plasma samples were challenging to pipette in terms of the samples 
being rather turbid. Plasma samples are the clear phase of a centrifuged whole blood sample, which 
had been treated with anti-coagulant. However, the samples could possibly have possessed some 
fibrinogen which had initiated to form clots that made accurate pipetting a challenging procedure. 
This could possibly have led to uneven pipetting of plasma samples, or potentially other particles in 
the plasma samples might have inhibited the antibody binding and thereby decreased the assay sen-
sitivity. 
 
The rest of the data had A450 values within the range of detection, and the majority of the dupli-
cates have aligned data. The bar diagrams in Figure 7.13, Figure 7.14, and Figure 7.15 visualize the 
determined ratio of OC per total protein of each sample and creates a tissue specific overview be-
tween lean and obese variants of the respective tissue samples.

79 
In Figure 7.15, the OC concentrations across the obese and lean SAT samples do not differ much. 
Although, the obese pig 25 does possess the highest amount of OC in comparison to any of the 
other SAT samples. At the same time, it is seen that the second obese sample from pig 13 possess 
the lowest amount of OC across both lean and obese samples. This indicates absence of consistency 
in the ratio across the obese samples, leading to a lack of pattern, which creates inconclusive data 
whether the amount of OC is affected or not between lean and obese SAT samples.  
 
Overall, the results obtained from the ELISA did not suggest that the amount of OC differs between 
lean and obese SAT, heart, or liver tissue. Moreover, it is not guaranteed that atherosclerotic lesions 
are found in the obese Ossabaw pigs, which otherwise could have given rise to different levels of 
OC in obese and lean tissue. However, in the RT-qPCR results it seems like the obese tissue have 
regulated expression levels of MGP, which indicates that atherosclerotic lesions might have devel-
oped in the obese pigs. Another reason behind the consistent level of OC between lean and obese 
pigs in this study could be reasoned by the fact that these pigs are fed with a MetS induced diet in 
short-term. It is possible that the potential atherosclerotic plaque formation are too early in terms of 
cells transdifferentiating to find a difference in protein levels of OC yet.  
Furthermore, the porcine OC ELISA kit applied in this thesis does not distinguish between carbox-
ylated and uncarboxylated OC. This may also mean that there could be an undetected variance in 
the activity of OC between lean and obese SAT, liver, heart tissue, and plasma. Potentially, the ac-
tivity of OC could have been investigated if kits targeting human cOC and human ucOC were ap-
plied, since human and porcine OC possess 86.0% identical aas. Thereby it could have been investi-
gated whether the degree of carboxylation of OC could have led to cOC or ucOC being a potential 
biomarker, even though the total amount of OC possibly was consistent between lean and obese tis-
sue.  
Lastly, it is worth mentioning that the number of samples obtained from 4 lean pigs and 2 obese 
pigs utilized in the ELISA does not represent a large enough population to create a sufficient 
amount of data which can be used to draw robust conclusions. The same applies for the RT-qPCR 
results, where cDNA were created from tissue obtained from 4 lean pigs and 3 obese. Therefore, it 
is not possible to reject the possibility that the amount of OC in SAT, liver, and heart tissue, poten-
tially also plasma, might vary between lean and obese tissue. Moreover, the tendencies in regulation 
of expression discussed in the RT-qPCR results are solely indicative and cannot draw any robust 
conclusions. 
 
 
 
==PAGE:81==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
80 
8 Conclusion 
This thesis set out to investigate the regulation of the VKD Gla-proteins MGP, OC and UCMA in 
relation to obesity-induced atherosclerosis using the obese Ossabaw pig model. In addition, OC was 
examined as a potential biomarker for the detection of atherosclerosis.  
  
The RT-qPCR provided RQ results of the target DNA, hence the RQ was used as an indication of 
the degree of expression in the examined samples. The PCR products obtained from the assays con-
ducted with designed primers targeting OC and UCMA cDNA were unspecific, consequently the 
data was invalid and the regulation of OC and UCMA expression could not be examined in this the-
sis. The PCR products from the assays targeting MGP cDNA were specific and indicated that obe-
sity affected the regulation of MGP expression in a tissue-specific manner. The high expression of 
MGP in the obese SAT and aorta samples, compared to lean controls indicated local upregulation of 
MGP due to obesity. The decreased MGP expression in obese PAX samples, compared to the lean 
controls, indicated that obesity downregulated MGP systemically in response to obesity. The found 
MGP expression in the liver samples differed between the assays utilizing different primers which 
targeted MGP DNA, which led to inconclusive assumptions whether obesity had a regulatory effect 
on MGP expression in liver tissue. The difference in regulation of MGP expression between obese 
and lean heart tissue remain unclear and has to be further investigated. 
 
The results obtained from the ELISA did not provide any significant difference in OC protein levels 
between the lean and obese samples of plasma, SAT, heart, or liver tissue extractions. The ELISA 
results in this thesis did not indicate that OC would be a strong biomarker detecting developing ath-
erosclerosis. However, the difference in the fractions of cOC and ucOC within lean and obese tissue 
remain unknown and might have to be investigated further. 
 
Overall, the results obtained from this indicates that MGP expression is regulated in a tissue-spe-
cific manner in response to obesity in the obese Ossabaw pig model, while the regulation of OC and 
UCMA could not be evaluated in this study. In addition, the findings in this thesis did not indicate 
OC to be a proper biomarker to detect atherosclerosis. 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
==PAGE:82==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
81 
9 Future Perspectives  
The findings in this master thesis found MGP to be regulated in a tissue-specific manner in response 
to obesity. MGP is normally expressed in VSMCs, however the findings in this thesis suggest that 
further investigation in the regulation of MGP in specific tissue would be relevant in order to under-
stand the mechanisms behind potential atherosclerosis development in obesity models. Additional 
investigation could comprise ELISA or immunohistochemistry assays targeting dp-ucMGP & p-
cMGP to examine the activity of MGP as calcification inhibitor in the specific tissue that had shown 
up- or downregulating behavior of MGP expression in response to obesity. However, since MGP 
normally is expressed by VSMCs but the findings in this thesis indicates that obesity induces the 
expression of MGP in SAT, then it might be relevant to gain insight in which cells are responsible 
for expressing them within SAT. Potentially immunofluorescent assays targeting MGP and adipo-
cytes could provide insight In whether adipocytes express MGP within SAT. 
 
To determine if OC and UCMA expression were up- or downregulated it could be relevant to de-
sign new primers targeting other locations in the genomic sequence or adjust the PCR conditions. 
However, it could be relevant to investigate if there might be splice site variants expressed differ-
ently across tissue. This could possibly create a better foundation in order to design primer pairs 
that target splice site variants of OC and UCMA specific for the examined tissue in an RT-qPCR 
assay.   
 
The results from the ELISA targeting both cOC and ucOC protein levels did not suggest any find-
ings of significant biological difference in the protein levels within lean and obese plasma obtained 
from whole blood or SAT, liver, and heart tissue. However, it is relevant to note that the ELISA did 
not take the degree of carboxylation into account, which might differ between lean and obese tissue. 
It might be relevant to examine the fraction of cOC and ucOC in lean and obese tissue encompass-
ing the same amount of OC. The fraction of carboxylated OC might differ in response to obesity, 
hence potential development of atherosclerosis. This could be investigated by ELISA or immuno-
histochemistry targeting the abovementioned cOC and ucOC.  
  
Lastly, the findings in this thesis are based on tissue samples obtained from 4 lean pigs and 3 obese 
pigs. To obtain results that allow for robust and statistically significant conclusions, a larger number 
of pigs and tissue samples would be required.

12.3 Appendix 3 
Guidelines for qPCR primer design for gene expression analysis 
(mRNA) 
 
1. Open two tabs in browser: 1) Ensembl Genome Browser - http://www.ensembl.org/, 2) Primer 
3 - https://bioinfo.ut.ee/primer3-0.4.0/ (Primer 3 URL sometimes changes, google “Primer 3” 
if this one is outdated)  
2. In Ensembl: ”Search:” drop-down menu, choose relevant species – “for”, type gene of interest. 
Press Go. Select gene from results list (typically top result) 
 
 
 
 
3. Check table, this will show how many splice variants exists – hopefully only one, otherwise 
you have to identify regions that are identical across splice variants if you want primers to 
amplify all splice variants 
 
There are three splice variants in this example, but they differ only in the 5’ end, so it should 
be possible to identify primers that will amplify all splice variants 
 
 
4. Click on relevant link under Transcript ID in the table – in this example it could be any of 
them. 
==PAGE:98==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
97 
5. Left-hand menu (close to the bottom), click “Export data”. Output format should be FASTA. 
Select only cDNA to be exported. No need to change other settings. Click “Next” 
6. Export format: choose “Text”. New tab will open; the top sequence will be the cDNA se-
quence (identifier includes “cdna:protein_coding”). Copy/paste this sequence to Primer 3 
 
 
7. Go back to Ensembl tab, left-hand menu, click on “Exons” 
8. Rules of thumb for designing forward primer: 
a. keep 3’ end GC rich, approx. 3 out of 6 bases at the end should be G or C. 
b. design primers that amplify sequences close to the 3’ end of cDNA – most reverse 
transcription methods employ oligo(dT) primers (that anneal to the poly(A) tail of 
mRNA) to prime the synthesis of cDNA, so the 3’ end of mRNA is generally more 
likely to be successfully reverse transcribed. If the reverse transcription also employs 
random primers (which could possibly anneal anywhere to the mRNA), one can be 
less strict regarding staying close to the 3’ end. 
c. firstly try to design a forward primer over the first intron, i.e.  half of the primer on 
either side of the intron. If not possible, design a primer on the 5’ side of an intron and 
have Primer 3 pick a reverse primer on the 3’ side, so that the product includes an 
intron. In this case, remember to check where the reverse primer is located; sometimes 
Primer 3 will pick a reverse primer that is partially located in two exons (like the over-
intron design – this is good, and the primer name should thus indicate “oi” rather than 
“i”, see naming instructions below). If this also fails, move on to the next intron and 
try and design a forward primer over this intron. 
d. when deciding whether to try and design an over-intron primer or an including-intron 
primer type, generally chose the type that allows you to stay the closest to the 3’ end. 
e. when designing two primer pairs for the same gene, make sure that the products do 
not overlap – or at least make sure to make a note of it if it cannot be avoided. 
9. Forward primer design example, over-intron: pick approx. half of the primer sequence (3’ 
half) on the 3’ side of an exon: 
==PAGE:99==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
98 
 
 
Then pick the 5’ half of the primer from the 5’ side of the exon: 
 
 
Combine sequences as input in Primer 3 as forward (left) primer: 
 
 
Leave field for reverse (right) primer empty and let Primer 3 define this. Make sure that 
thermodynamics settings is set to Breslauer et al. 1986. 
 
 
Change a few of the setting before continuing, product size range should be 80-120, Primer 
Tm should be Min = 59.0, Opt = 60.0, Max = 61.0 – this is primarily important when de-
signing assays for the BioMark (Fluidigm) platform, as all assays should then have same Tm 
and similar amplicon sizes ensures that different targets will be amplified with similar effi-
ciency. If using a different (more conventional) qPCR platform, then these rules are less im-
portant. 
 
 
 
10. Click ”Pick Primers”. Adjust primer length and GC content until Tm is acceptable and no 
secondary structures are predicted. Primer 3 will suggest a primer pair and show you where 
in the sequence it has defined the reverse primer 
==PAGE:100==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
99 
 
 
 
11. Assign a name to the primer pair that includes organism, gene designation, primer design, 
and product size. E.g. in the example above we designed primers the porcine (Sus scrofa, 
ssc) LIPE gene, where one primer is designed over an intron (oi), and it yields an 83 nt prod-
uct and the primers should therefore be named ssc_LIPE_oi_83_F and ssc_LIPE_oi_83_R, 
respectively.  
12. Over-intron primers are preferred as they can only amplify cDNA and not genomic DNA. 
However, if it is impossible to design acceptable over-intron primers, then design a primer 
pair where forward and reverse primers are places on either side of an exon. If the assay is 
amplifying contaminating genomic DNA, then this will be distinguishable as a secondary 
peak in the melt curve analysis. Example on including-intron primer pair for the ssc LIPE 
gene: 
Define a forward primer close to the intron and copy this to Primer 3: 
 
 
Let Primer 3 pick the reverse primer like before; if you have placed the forward primer close 
enough to the intron then the reverse primer will placed on the other side of the intron. Pri-
mer 3 output will show you primer sequences, product size, etc. and also show where the 
reverse primer anneals: 
==PAGE:101==
Laura-Christine Astrupgaard (s193766) 
Master Thesis 
Investigation of Regulation of Gla Proteins during Atherosclerosis Development in The Obese Ossabaw Pig Model 
 
100 
 
 
 
Naming of these primers will then be ssc_LIPE_i_117_F and ssc_LIPE_i_117_R, respec-
tively. 
 
13. Some genes don’t have introns. In those cases you just paste the cDNA sequence into Primer 
3 and let it pick both forward and reverse primers. If you are designing more than one primer 
pair for an intron-less gene then make sure that you pick assays from Primer 3 that amplify 
non-overlapping amplicons.
